# Guidebook for Accessible Design

## About This Guidebook

This guidebook provides design specifications grounded in the best available evidence for making built environments accessible to the full range of human functional capacity. It covers new construction, major refurbishment, and post-occupancy adaptation across residential, commercial, healthcare, educational, hospitality, and cultural building types. It is written for architects, access consultants, interior designers, occupational therapists, project managers, developers, housing authorities, and anyone responsible for decisions about the built environment.

**The central principle:** Building codes represent legal minimums. This guidebook operates above the code floor throughout, calibrated to Tier 1 occupational therapy clinical research and the best available multilingual evidence. Meeting code is a necessary but completely insufficient condition for producing an accessible environment.

## Companion Volumes

This guidebook is accompanied by supplementary resources that extend coverage beyond the three-volume core:

- **Designing for Different Body Sizes: Supplementary Volume** — Covers large body size (formerly BAR), short stature (LPA), children/adolescents (CHD), and extremely tall individuals (EXH). *Included in this document — see Supplementary Volume at end of Appendices.*

# Volume I: Foundations, Categories, Synthesis

## Part 1: Foundations of Accessible Design

### §1.1 Definitions:

**What is Accessibility?** Accessibility is the condition in which any person — regardless of physical, sensory, cognitive, or psychological characteristics — can independently, safely, and with dignity reach, enter, use, and exit a built environment and all its facilities.

This definition has three operative components:

**Independence.**

The person does not require another person's physical assistance to navigate or use the environment. Accessible design removes environmental barriers that create dependency. A well-designed accessible bathroom, for example, allows a wheelchair user to transfer, shower, and dress without assistance from a carer — which has profound implications for privacy, autonomy, and the economics of care.

**Safety.**

The environment does not create hazards specific to the person's impairment. A wet room floor that drains incorrectly is not merely inconvenient — it creates a fall hazard that can be catastrophic for a person with reduced balance. A fire alarm that delivers only an audio signal is not merely inadequate — it is life-threatening for a deaf occupant. Accessible design is, in significant part, safety design.

**Dignity.**

The accessible route, feature, or facility does not stigmatise, other, or degrade the person using it. A separate 'accessible entrance' at the back of a building next to a service area is a dignity failure even if it meets dimensional compliance. Accessible features that are aesthetically indistinguishable from the mainstream environment serve users without marking them as different. Functional access extends beyond physical dimensions.

True accessibility encompasses:

- **Physical access:** step-free routes, clear widths, turning space, lever hardware, reach zones
- **Sensory access:** visual contrast, tactile information, hearing loops, visual fire alarms, vibrotactile alerting
- **Cognitive access:** predictable layouts, clear wayfinding, plain language, sensory calm
- **Communicative access:** hearing loop coverage, captioning, pictogram signage, interpreter seating
- **Temporal access:** provisions remain accessible throughout the full range of time that people occupy the building
- **Emergency access:** evacuation provisions for all building users, not only ambulant non-disabled occupants (see Appendix D)

### Key Terms

**Impairment:** a condition of the body or mind. An impairment is a characteristic of the person.

**Disability:** the mismatch between a person's impairment and an environment that has not been designed to accommodate it. Disability is a characteristic of the person-environment interaction, not of the person alone.

**Accessible design / inclusive design:** design that eliminates or reduces the mismatch between impairment and environment, restoring function, independence, and dignity.

**Universal design:** design that works for the widest possible range of users without adaptation or specialist provision.

**DAR (Design for Adaptable Readiness):** construction-stage provisions that allow future accessibility modifications at minimal cost, without structural intervention. See §1.6.

**OT (Occupational Therapist):** a registered healthcare professional whose discipline is concerned with the relationship between person, environment, and occupation. OT is the highest-tier evidence source for accessible design in this document. See §1.5.

### §1.2 The Code-as-Floor Principle

Building codes represent legal minimums — not design targets, not expressions of best practice, and not statements about what constitutes a good environment for disabled people. This document operates above the code floor throughout. Statutory requirements are referenced as baselines only (Evidence Tier 6). Where codes conflict with higher-tier evidence, higher-tier evidence governs. The following multilingual endorsements of the code-as-floor principle confirm that this position is not a minority view, but the unanimous position of every major national accessibility body and advocacy organisation reviewed across 17 jurisdictions:

The logical implication: meeting building code is a necessary but completely insufficient condition for producing an accessible environment. Every specification in this document is calibrated above code, based on the best available evidence, primarily Tier 1 occupational therapy clinical research.

### §1.3 Designing for the Individual: Functional Capacity Over Category Minimums

A critical failing of code-based accessibility is the implicit assumption that all disabled people within a category have identical functional characteristics. ADA provisions specify dimensions for a notional 'wheelchair user' — a statistical construct that does not describe any actual person. This document rejects the category-minimum approach.

The governing principle is: **design must accommodate the actual functional capacity of the actual user, established through OT assessment, not the assumed functional capacity of the category.**

**Population variation within categories:** A person with a C4 spinal cord injury has very different functional capacities from a person with MS-related fatigue who uses a wheelchair only for long distances. Both are 'wheelchair users' under code classification. They require substantially different environments for genuine independence.

**Bidirectional variation:** Within any disability category, some users have greater functional capacity than the code assumption; others have lesser. Code design serves neither. An environment designed from OT assessment of actual functional capacity — with DAR provisions for declining capacity — serves both.

**Multiple simultaneous categories:** Most people acquire multiple impairments over time. A person who is a wheelchair user with MS (MOB + NEU), low vision (VIS), and early cognitive change (DEM) requires a design synthesis across all four categories simultaneously. See Part III for multiple disability category guidance.

**Temporal variation:** Functional capacity fluctuates. MS, PAIN, and CFS/ME conditions produce good days and bad days. PCS produces hour-to-hour variation in cognitive function. An accessible environment must remain accessible across the full range of the individual's functional variation.

### Working with Occupational Therapists in Design

Occupational Therapists (OTs) are the trained professionals whose discipline directly addresses the person-environment-occupation relationship. An OT assessment of a specific person in a specific environment produces: a precise characterisation of the person's functional capacities; a precise identification of the environmental barriers for that person; a prioritised intervention list; and post-modification outcome verification.

For residential and specialist environments, OT involvement is not optional best practice — it is the mechanism by which functional-capacity-based design becomes achievable. In new construction and major refurbishment, OT involvement at design stage produces specifications that eliminate barriers before they are built.

**Co-design requirement.**

OT assessment alone does not fulfil the co-design obligation in CRPD Article 4.3. Best practice in accessible design involves disabled people as active co-designers — not only as clinical subjects or end-user consultees. Where possible, design teams should engage disabled people with lived experience of the relevant condition(s) in design review at schematic and technical design stages. This is not a substitute for OT assessment; it is a complementary process that identifies barriers the clinical evidence base may not have captured.

### §1.4 Universal Design, Inclusive Design, and This Guidebook

#### §1.4.1 Universal Design Is a Floor, Not a Goal

Universal design (Mace, Hardie \& Place, 1991) is the design philosophy that environments should work for the widest possible range of users without adaptation or specialist provision. Its seven principles — equitable use, flexibility, simple and intuitive use, perceptible information, tolerance for error, low physical effort, and size and space for approach and use — define the standard from which all design in the built environment begins.

In Canada and across most jurisdictions represented in this guidebook, universal design principles are substantively integrated into statutory building codes. Universal design is therefore co-extensive with code compliance. A building that satisfies UD criteria has met the legal minimum. It has not achieved best practice, and it has not achieved inclusive design.

Universal design produces a single solution calibrated to an abstracted, averaged, anonymous user. It sets fixed values derived from population statistics rather than from the functional capacity of the people who will actually use the building. For buildings where no specific user population can be identified or anticipated, this is the appropriate and sufficient starting point. For every other building, it is only the floor.

#### §1.4.2 Inclusive Design: The Aspiration of This Guidebook

Inclusive design (BS 7000-6:2005; RIBA, 2022) is the aspiration and method of this guidebook. It is not a fallback from universal design, and it is not a compromise. It is the positive commitment to designing as well as possible for the actual people who will use a building, informed by the best available evidence about their functional capacities.

The core principle of inclusive design, as applied in this guidebook, is this: the more specifically you can identify who will use a building, the greater your obligation to move from population averages toward functional capacity design for those people. Specificity of knowledge creates specificity of obligation.

This guidebook is written for Tier 1 and Tier 2 practice. All specifications, evidence tables, and design guidance in this document are inclusive design instruments — tools for going beyond the code floor toward the best that evidence and design can offer.

#### §1.4.3 The Three-Tier Hierarchy

This guidebook applies a three-tier design hierarchy. DAR (Design for Adaptable Readiness, §1.6) is mandatory at every tier without exception.

|  **Tier**    |  **Standard**                               |  **Context**                                                                                |  **Specification   form**                                                                                                              |  **DAR**   |
| --------------- | ---------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------- |
|  **Tier 0**  |  **Universal  Design / Code Compliance**    |  No specific  population can be anticipated. Averaged, anonymous user.                       |  Range of values calibrated to an abstractly disabled non-existent person.                                                                            |  Mandatory  |
|  **Tier 1**  |  **Population-Informed  Inclusive Design**  |  Identifiable  disability populations among regular users. This guidebook's primary domain.  |  Ranges.  Median is the population-informed default. Range encodes functional capacity  distribution across the identified population.  |  Mandatory  |
|  **Tier 2**  |  **Person-Specific  Inclusive Design**      |  Individual  occupant or named user is known. OT assessment available.                       |  Position  within the Tier 1 range, resolved by the individual's functional capacity  assessment.                                      |  Mandatory  |

**Tier 0 — Universal Design / Code Compliance ​**is the appropriate standard only where no specific user population can be identified or reasonably anticipated. It uses single fixed specification values calibrated to a statistical average user. Even at Tier 0, DAR is mandatory: future occupants cannot be anticipated at construction stage, and construction-stage provisions must preserve the building's ability to accommodate future accessibility modifications.

**Tier 1 — Population-Informed Inclusive Design ​**is the primary domain of this guidebook. It applies wherever an identifiable disability population can be reasonably anticipated among the building's regular users. The overwhelming majority of buildings fall into this tier: every public institution, workplace, health facility, transit hub, educational building, housing development, and civic space will serve people across the MOB, VIS, NEU, DEM, NDV, BAR, OFS and related population categories.

*At Tier 1, specifications are expressed as ranges.* The range is derived from the functional capacity distribution across the identified population. The median of the range is the population-informed default — the best single value for a designer or practitioner working without a specific individual's assessment. The full range exists because the population is not homogeneous: functional capacity varies within every disability category, and the specification must accommodate that variation.

**Tier 2 — Person-Specific Inclusive Design ​**applies where the individual occupant or primary user is known: a residential commission for a named person, a workplace adjustment for a specific employee, or a specialist care environment designed around a defined client. At Tier 2, the practitioner's task is not to select the median value but to determine where within the Tier 1 range the specification should land, based on that individual's functional capacity assessment, OT findings, and personal preferences.

The individual's own functional capacity is the answer. The range is the envelope within which that answer sits. *Tier 2 does not require different specifications from Tier 1 — it resolves the Tier 1 range to a specific value for a specific person.*

#### §1.4.4 The Specification Range as Design Mechanism

The ranges appearing throughout Part 7 of this guidebook are not expressions of uncertainty or imprecision. They are the designed-in mechanism that allows a single specification item to serve both Tier 1 and Tier 2 practice simultaneously.

**At Tier 1: ​**use the median of the stated range as the population-informed default. The median represents the best-evidence value for the identified population in the absence of individual assessment data.

**At Tier 2: ​**use the individual's OT functional capacity assessment to locate the appropriate position within the range. The person's own capabilities, preferences, and functional variation over time determine the specific value.

**The range boundary: ​**values outside the stated range fall outside the evidence base for the identified population. Departures from the range require documented clinical justification.

Wherever a range appears in Part 7, the following framing applies: the range is the Tier 1 population envelope; the median is the Tier 1 default; the specific value within the range is determined by Tier 2 OT assessment. This framing should be treated as implicit throughout the guidebook and is made explicit in Part 7 items where the clinical stakes are highest.

#### §1.4.5 Why Universal Design Alone Is Insufficient

Universal design produces a single solution optimised for a statistical average across the widest possible population. This guidebook operates above that standard for reasons inherent to its scope.

**Fixed values vs. functional capacity ranges. ​**

A single fixed value cannot accommodate the functional capacity variation within any disability population. UD's fixed-value approach produces specifications that are correct for the average and wrong for everyone distributed across the population's actual range.

**Temporal and conditional variation. ​**

Many populations in this guidebook — OFS/ME, OFS/POTS, NDV/ADHD, PAIN, NEU/PCS — involve fluctuating capacity. A design calibrated to a single fixed value will fail these users on their high-impairment days. The Tier 1 range accommodates this variation; UD's fixed value does not.

**Populations absent from UD's historical base.​**

Universal design was developed primarily against a physical disability and ageing population. The BAR, DBL, and OFS categories in this guidebook have no adequate provision in standard UD frameworks. Population-specific evidence overrides UD assumptions for these categories.

**Irresolvable multi-population specification conflicts. ​**

Some optimal values for one population are contraindicated for another. High-contrast visual environments support VIS/vis users; they can trigger sensory overload for some NDV/SENS users. UD offers no principled resolution. This guidebook addresses these conflicts through explicit conflict notation in Part 7 items, with evidence-based guidance for each.

The aspiration of universality — that environments should work for everyone — is achieved through rigorous population-specific inclusive design, not through adherence to a design philosophy calibrated to an averaged user.

#### §1.4.6 Application to Residential Design

New residential construction where the eventual occupant is unknown at construction stage operates at Tier 0 as its legal baseline. DAR is mandatory above that baseline, since future occupants will include people with disabilities whose specific needs cannot be anticipated. The Lifetime Homes standard (Joseph Rowntree Foundation, 1999) and CAN/ASC 2.8:2025 represent the minimum DAR threshold for speculative residential construction; both are below the best-practice standard set by this guidebook.

Where an individual occupant is known — a custom build, an adaptations commission, or a specialist residential environment — Tier 2 applies. Design is calibrated to that person's documented functional profile and OT assessment. DAR remains mandatory to preserve adaptability for future occupants and for changes in the current occupant's functional capacity over time.

**Key references: ​**Mace, Hardie \& Place (1991); BS 7000-6:2005; RIBA (2022); CAN/ASC 2.8:2025; Joseph Rowntree Foundation Lifetime Homes (1999); Imrie (2012); Hamraie (2017).

### §1.5 Evidence Hierarchy

The following tier structure governs source prioritisation throughout this document. Where sources conflict, higher-tier evidence governs regardless of jurisdiction, language, or number of citations.

**Tier 1 — Occupational Therapy Clinical Research (Highest Authority)**

OT evidence is intervention-tested: specifications are derived from observation of actual people performing actual tasks in actual environments, with outcome measurement. OT evidence sits at Tier 1 because it is directly person-environment-occupation tested, calibrated to actual functional capacity ranges, and intervention-verified. *Primary Tier 1 sources:* CAOT (2018); JOTA (2022); COTEC/WFOT position statements; AOTA (2023); Housing Enabler (Iwarsson, Lund University; r = 0.74 reliability); RCOT (2019).

**Co-primary Tier 1 — Lived Experience and Participatory Design Research**

In alignment with CRPD Article 4.3, which requires States Parties to actively involve disabled people in developing accessibility standards and policies, this guidebook treats the documented lived expertise of disabled people as co-primary evidence alongside OT clinical research. Where lived experience evidence and clinical research findings align, confidence is elevated. Where they diverge, both are presented and the divergence is noted. Participatory design processes — where disabled people are involved as co-designers, not only as research subjects --- are treated as a form of evidence generation. *Primary co-Tier 1 sources:* The Kelsey IDS co-design methodology; Gallaudet DeafSpace participatory design process; Motionspot neuroinclusive design co-design protocols; patient-led research collectives including ME Association (UK) and Patient Led Research

**Tier 2 — NGO and Advocacy Organisation Best-Practice Guidelines**

NGO and advocacy guidelines are developed by and for disabled communities. They incorporate lived experience expertise — a form of validity that clinical and technical standards frequently lack. *Primary Tier 2 sources:* CNIB (Canada); Alzheimer Society / BrainXchange (Canada); DeafSpace Project, Gallaudet University (USA); HLAA (USA); The Kelsey IDS (USA); Motionspot (UK); NAS (UK); APF France Handicap; CERMI; ONCE Foundation (Spain); CAWaB; SgEnable (Singapore); KODDI (South Korea); DbI Nordic programme; Sense UK. Collaborative (USA/UK).

**Co-primary Tier 2 — Occupational Therapy Clinical Practice Guidelines**

**Tier 3 — Systematic Reviews and Meta-Analyses**

*Primary Tier 3 sources:* Bettarello et al. (2021); Marquardt (2011); Koontz et al. (2012); Ulrich (1984, 1991); Petaccia et al. (2021); Wellecke et al. (2022); Sherrington et al. (2019); Räty et al. (2021).

**Tier 4 — International Standards with Evidence Basis**

CRPD Article 9 (UN, 2006) — 185 States Parties; foundational international legal framework for accessibility. PAS 6463:2022 (BSI, UK). WELL v2 (2024). ISO 23599:2019 — tactile walking surface indicators. IEC 60118-4:2014+AMD1:2017 — hearing loop performance [UK/EU: BS EN IEC 60118-4:2015+A1:2018]. ISO 21542:2021 — building accessibility and usability. EN 17210:2021 — pan-European accessibility.

**Tier 5 — National Beyond-Code Frameworks**

BS 8300:2018 (UK); The Kelsey IDS (USA, 2023); ABCB Silver/Gold/Platinum Livable Housing (Australia); RHFAC v4.0 (Canada, 2024); NS 11001-1:2018 (Norway); DIN 18040-1/2/3 (Germany); BCA Universal Design Index (Singapore); BF certification framework (South Korea); SBi-anvisning 230 (Denmark); Esteettömyysopas / ESKEH (Finland).

**Tier 6 — Statutory Codes (Reference Baseline Only)**

Legal minimum. Design that meets code only is not best practice and in many cases produces environments that remain inaccessible to significant proportions of disabled users.

### §1.6 The DAR Principle — Design for Adaptable Readiness

Design for Adaptable Readiness (DAR) is the principle that construction-stage decisions should preserve the building's ability to accommodate future accessibility modifications at minimal cost. The most expensive accessible features to retrofit are negligible to incorporate at the schematic design stage.

**Policy implication:** DAR provision in new construction is the highest-return accessible design investment available. Building codes that do not require DAR provisions impose these multiplied costs on ageing homeowners and disability communities.

### §1.7 CRPD Framework Alignment

All provisions in this guidebook operate within the framework of Article

9 of the UN Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (CRPD, 2006). CRPD Article 9 establishes accessibility of the built environment as a legal obligation for 185 States Parties. The general principles of Article 3 (dignity, autonomy, non-discrimination, participation) provide the normative framework within which technical specifications are derived.

### §1.8 Evidence Frameworks

The following theoretical frameworks underpin the evidence basis for design specifications throughout this document. Understanding these frameworks allows readers to assess the strength and scope of each specification.

**§1.8.1 Biomechanical Frame of Reference** Specifications derived from this framework address physical capacity, joint range of motion, muscle strength, grip, reach, balance, and gait. Grab bar diameters, handrail heights, turning radii, and threshold heights are biomechanically derived. All Part 7 specifications grounded in this framework are calibrated to the functional range of the relevant disability category, not to code averages.

**§1.8.2 Dunn's Sensory Processing Model** Sensory processing differences produce four profiles: sensory seeking, sensory avoiding, sensory sensitivity, and low registration. Design must accommodate all four. Acoustic specifications (Category A), lighting specifications (Category B), and colour/surface specifications (Category C) are calibrated across all four profiles.

**§1.8.3 Ecology of Human Performance (EHP) Framework** EHP defines four intervention strategies: establish, alter, adapt, prevent. Accessible design interventions in Part 7 are classified by EHP strategy: most interventions alter the context (environment) rather than the person; some prevent barrier formation at source.

**§1.8.4 Compensatory Frame of Reference** Where functional capacity cannot be restored, the environment compensates. Lever hardware, automatic doors, and grab bars are compensatory provisions.

**§1.8.5 Allen's Cognitive Disabilities Model (CDM)** CDM identifies six cognitive levels (0--6). Environmental specifications for DEM and NDV users are calibrated to the cognitive level range of the target population. Wayfinding, signage, and spatial layout specifications (Category D) draw on CDM research.

**§1.8.6 Person-Environment-Occupation-Performance (PEOP) Model** PEOP frames the person-environment relationship as a dynamic interaction affecting occupation performance. Dignified access, socially integrated design, and equivalent provisions are PEOP requirements — not merely aesthetic preferences.

**§1.8.7 Ecological Psychology — Affordance Theory** Gibson's affordance theory holds that the environment communicates its usability through physical cues. Accessible environments provide affordances legible to the full range of functional capacities. Grab bar shapes, door pull widths, and floor texture specifications are affordance-based.

**§1.8.8 Competence-Press Model** Lawton \& Nahemow's (1973) model holds that adaptive behaviour occurs in a zone of moderate environmental press relative to individual competence. Specifications for DEM, CFS/ME, and OFS users are calibrated to maintain press within the performance zone for the lower end of the functional capacity range.

**§1.8.9 Attention Restoration Theory (ART) and Stress Recovery Theory (SRT)** ART and SRT provide the evidence basis for biophilic design provisions (Appendix B (Biophilic Design)), natural lighting specifications (B-09), and sensory retreat provisions (A-16, F-01).

### §1.9 Scope

This guidebook applies to: new residential construction; major residential refurbishments; non-residential buildings including healthcare, education, hospitality, commercial, and cultural facilities; and post-occupancy adaptation of existing buildings. It applies to any building intended for occupation by people with any of the disability categories defined in Part II. It does not apply to: minor works (single-trade interventions under £5,000); temporary structures with an intended lifespan under 6 months; vehicles. Where this document conflicts with statutory requirements, comply with the statutory requirement and document the conflict as a Tier 6 baseline limitation. Where this document requires more than statutory requirements, this document governs. *Next: Part 2 — Disability Categories* *Cross-reference: Part 3 (Multiple Categories); Part 8 (Conflict Resolutions); Part 12 (Economics and DAR costs)*
## Part 2: Disability Categories

Thirteen disability categories are addressed in this document. These are not exhaustive clinical classifications — they are design-relevant groupings of people with similar environmental needs and barriers. One person may belong to multiple categories simultaneously. See Part 3 for multiple disability category design guidance and Part 8 for conflict resolutions.

### Category Code Reference

| Code | Category |
| --- |
| MOB | Mobility \& Strength |
| UPL | Upper Limb \& Amputation |
| VIS | Vision Impairment |
| DEAF | Deaf / Hard of Hearing |
| NEU | Neurological \& Acquired Brain Injury |
| DEM | Dementia |
| NDV | Neurodivergence |
| NDV/MH | Mental Health / PTSD / Trauma (sub-code of NDV) |
| PAIN | Chronic Pain / Fibromyalgia / Migraine |
| DBL | DeafBlind |
| OFS | Orthostatic \& Fatigue Spectrum |
| IntD | Intellectual Disability |
| ALL | All disability categories |

---

### §2.1 MOB — Mobility \& Strength

**Definition**

Physical access, ambulation, and postural needs arising from lower limb impairment, generalised weakness, or fatigue. Includes: wheelchair users (manual and powered); walker, cane, crutch, and rollator users; ambulatory mobility impairment (gait dysfunction, hip/knee replacement, paraplegia/hemiplegia, cerebral palsy — motor); generalised strength and endurance limitations.

**Core environmental needs**

- Step-free routes throughout; maximum ramp gradient 1:20
- Turning space ≥1500 mm diameter at all decision points
- Corridor width ≥1200 mm (single); ≥1500 mm (passing)
- Door clear width ≥900 mm (minimum accessible); ≥915 mm preferred for new construction; ≥1000 mm on all bariatric-accessible routes
- Opening force ≤22 N
- Reach zone 400–1200 mm from floor
- Seating at 450–500 mm seat height with armrests throughout
- Rest seating every 20–30 m on primary routes

**Special Considerations — MOB**

Biomechanics: ramps above 1:12 require high-force shoulder technique unsustainable by many wheelchair users (Waters et al., 1988). Corridors below 900 mm width increase metabolic cost of wheelchair propulsion 30–45% (Koontz et al., 2012). Seat height of 380 mm increases hip extensor moment \~40% compared to 450 mm (Rodosky et al., 1989). ICF (WHO, 2001): environmental factors (e1) constitute either barriers or facilitators.

**Distinct from BAR:** MOB addresses the mechanics of mobility device use and ambulant access. Large body size design provisions (BAR) address structural load-bearing requirements and dignity provisions and have been relocated to the Supplementary Volume, Section IV. MOB specifications apply independently; where BAR provisions are also required, see Supplementary Volume.

**Distinct from OFS:** MOB provisions eliminate physical barriers to movement; OFS provisions eliminate exertion load on barrier-free routes. Rest seating (≤20 m, E-10) is the primary OFS-specific provision that MOB does not require as a universal minimum.

*OT Framework: Biomechanical Frame of Reference.*

*Evidence Base: Koontz et al. (2012); Rodosky et al. (1989); Waters et al. (1988).*

---

### §2.2 UPL — Upper Limb \& Amputation

UPL is consolidated as sub-code MOB/UPL in an earlier edition. Category K (Upper Limb Provisions, Part 7) is retained as a dedicated item set.

**Definition**

Design needs arising from impairment or absence of one or both upper limbs, or significantly reduced hand/wrist/arm function. Includes: unilateral and bilateral upper limb amputation; partial hand amputation; congenital limb difference; prosthetic users (body-powered, myoelectric, cosmetic); grip weakness from any cause (rheumatoid arthritis, osteoarthritis, MS-related tremor, stroke hemiplegia, Parkinson's disease, lupus, post-surgical immobilisation); single-hand operation requirement.

**Core environmental needs**

All hardware operable single-handed, without fine pinch grip:

- Door handles: lever, D-handle, or push plate only. No round knobs. Return tip on lever handles to prevent hook snagging.
- Taps/faucets: single-lever mixer only. Sensor-activated acceptable.
- Light switches: large rocker or push type. No small toggle.
- Controls: large surface, ≤22 N activation force.
- Grab bars: diameter 32–45 mm (circular cross-section preferred; oval cross-sections to achieve equivalent grip perimeter; avoid knurled surfaces). Bilateral provision — bars on both sides of all transfer locations — is essential.

*OT Framework: Biomechanical Frame of Reference.*

*Evidence Base: ISO 21542:2021; CSA B651:2023; BS 8300:2018.*

---

### §2.3 VIS — Vision Impairment

**Definition**

Sensory impairment affecting visual perception. Includes: blind and severely visually impaired; low vision. For combined vision and hearing loss see §2.10 DBL. For Deaf and hard of hearing see §2.4 DEAF.

**Core environmental needs**

- LRV contrast ≥30 at all critical junctions: floor/wall, stair nosing/tread, door frame/wall, grab bar/wall, handrail/wall
- Minimum 50 lux on accessible routes; minimum 300 lux in working areas
- TWSI (Tactile Walking Surface Indicators): directional bars for guidance, truncated domes for hazard warning, per ISO 23599:2019
- No glare (UGR <19); matte floor surfaces (<30 gloss units); gradual lighting transitions (no >10:1 illuminance change without transition zone)
- Signage: minimum 150 mm character height at 10 m viewing distance; sans-serif; high contrast

**Distinct from DBL:** VIS users have functional impairment in one primary sensory channel. DBL users have impairment in both vision and hearing simultaneously, requiring tactile-first redundancy that VIS provisions alone do not address.

*OT Framework: Compensatory Frame of Reference.*

*Evidence Base: ISO 23599:2019; BS 8300:2018.*

---

### §2.4 DEAF — Deaf / Hard of Hearing

**Definition**

Sensory impairment affecting auditory perception. Includes: profoundly deaf; hard of hearing; cochlear implant users; hearing aid users. Also includes members of the Deaf community — a distinct cultural and linguistic group for whom signed language is a primary language and Deaf identity is a cultural, not medical, designation. Design provisions should support visual communication channels and signed language use, not only audiological accommodation. See NAD (2023) position statement on Deaf cultural identity.

**Core environmental needs**

- Hearing loop installation (perimeter loop or counter/window loop) in all assembly and service spaces, compliant with IEC 60118-4:2014+AMD1:2017
- Visual fire alarms (strobes ≥110 cd) throughout; vibrating pagers for bedrooms
- Multi-modal alerts: no critical information conveyed by sound alone
- Room acoustics supporting speech intelligibility: STI ≥0.5; RT60 ≤0.6 s in communication spaces
- Clear sightlines for signed language interpretation; adequate lighting for facial expression and hand visibility
- Spatial layouts that support Deaf communication practices

**Distinct from DBL:** DEAF users have functional impairment in one primary sensory channel. DBL users have impairment in both vision and hearing simultaneously, requiring tactile-first redundancy that DEAF provisions alone do not address.

*OT Framework: Compensatory Frame of Reference.*

*Evidence Base: IEC 60118-4:2014+AMD1:2017; DeafSpace Design Guidelines (2010); BS 8300:2018; NAD (2023).*

---

### §2.5 NEU — Neurological \& Acquired Brain Injury

**Definition**

Neurological conditions affecting cognition, movement, sensation, fatigue, or behaviour, acquired after birth. Includes: TBI; post-concussion syndrome (PCS); ABI; stroke; Multiple Sclerosis (MS); Parkinson's disease; spinal cord injury.

**Core environmental needs**

- Reduced visual clutter and visual noise
- Reduced acoustic complexity (RT60 ≤0.6 s; no unnecessary background music)
- No fluorescent lighting (PCS photosensitivity); flicker-free LED throughout
- Lighting individually dimmable to minimum 50 lux
- Predictable, consistent spatial layout
- Rest seating every 20–30 m
- Clear toilet routing; accessible toilet within 20 m of all primary occupied spaces
- Thermal management: ambient temperature ≤18°C on primary circulation routes

**Special Considerations — MS**

MS is the most prevalent acquired neurological condition absent from existing accessibility standards. Approximately 2.9 million people worldwide live with MS (MSIF Atlas, 2023).

- Uhthoff's phenomenon: temporary worsening of neurological symptoms when core body temperature rises by approximately 0.5°C above individual baseline. Design environments for MS should target ambient temperatures of 18°C. See Part 8, §8.4.4.
- MS fatigue: disproportionate, not fully relieved by rest; fluctuates across the day. Design response: rest seating every 20–30 m; lifts ≤60 m from any point; even surfaces.
- MS bladder urgency: up to 80% of people with MS experience bladder problems. Accessible toilet within 20 m of all primary occupied spaces is a clinical priority.

**Distinct from NDV:** NEU conditions are acquired — they have a clinical onset event and may partially recover or stabilise. NDV is innate neurological variation with no recovery trajectory.

**Distinct from DEM:** NEU conditions may stabilise or partially recover; DEM follows progressive decline only.

*OT Framework: Ecology of Human Performance (EHP) — performance range concept.*

*Evidence Base: CAOT (2018); AOTA (2023); MSIF Atlas (2023); Rasminsky (1973); Cicerone et al. (2019).*

---

### §2.6 DEM — Dementia

Dementia is treated as a standalone category rather than a subset of NEU. Neurological conditions (NEU) may recover or stabilise; dementia follows progressive decline with no recovery. The research bases are distinct.

**Definition**

Dementia arising from progressive neurodegenerative conditions: Alzheimer's disease (\~60–70% of cases); vascular dementia; mixed dementia; Lewy Body Dementia (LBD); Frontotemporal Dementia (FTD).

**Core environmental needs**

- Wayfinding: high contrast at toilet doors (≥30 LRV); door colour-coding by function; memory boxes; landmark objects; no dead-ends (D-01 through D-09)
- Safety: secured exits where wandering is a risk; safe enclosed gardens; no scalding risk; non-slip floors
- Orientation: consistent spatial layout; clock-face visibility; daylight; familiar objects and domestic scale
- Sensory calm: low visual complexity; muted colour palette; acoustic calm
- Contrast: ≥30 LRV at toilet seat vs floor; ≤10 LRV between adjacent floor materials

**Special Considerations — LBD**

Patterned flooring, high-contrast geometric tiles, and strong directional shadows at floor level are associated with visual hallucinations in Lewy Body Dementia. In settings where LBD users are anticipated: specify plain, matte, uniform flooring throughout (C-06).

*OT Framework: Allen's Cognitive Disabilities Model (CDM).*

*Evidence Base: DSDC (2022); Marquardt (2011); Alzheimer's Disease International (2020); France Alzheimer (2022); Kirch \& Marquardt (2023).*

---

### §2.7 NDV — Neurodivergence

**Definition**

Innate neurological variation affecting sensory processing, attention, learning, communication, or social cognition. Distinct from dementia (acquired) and ABI (acquired). Includes: autism spectrum (AUT); ADHD; sensory processing differences (SENS); dyspraxia (DCD); misophonia (MIS); dyslexia.

**Core environmental needs**

- Sensory load management: RT60 ≤0.6 s in spaces where AUT users are expected; no HVAC hum above NC-25; muted, low-chroma colour palette; no flickering or strobing
- Sensory retreat: at least one quiet room or acoustic refuge accessible from primary circulation (A-16)
- Sensory graduation: transition from high-stimulation entry zones to low-stimulation work/rest areas (F-01)
- Predictability: consistent layout; no unexpected changes in route; visible destination from decision points
- Choice: both active/stimulating and contemplative/restful spaces available
- Individual control over lighting, temperature, and acoustic environment where possible (H-02)
- Signage: pictogram + single-word; no symbol-dense wayfinding; consistent placement throughout

*OT Framework: Dunn's Sensory Processing Model.*

*Evidence Base: Mostafa (2021); BSI PAS 6463:2022; Bettarello et al. (2021); RHFAC v4.0 (2024).*

---

### §2.8 NDV/MH — Mental Health, PTSD, and Trauma (Sub-code of NDV)

MH is consolidated as sub-code NDV/MH in an earlier edition. MH provisions are retained in full and are cross-coded throughout Part 7 as NDV/MH. Mental health categories include anxiety disorders, PTSD, depression, psychosis, and trauma-related conditions. These users are present in all building types, not only specialist mental health facilities.

**Definition**

Design needs arising from mental health conditions, trauma histories, and psychosocial disability. NDV/MH design prioritises environments that reduce threat perception, support autonomy and exit, and avoid non-clinical institutional aesthetics.

**Core environmental needs**

- Trauma-informed design: sightlines to exit from all seating positions; defensible space (back-to-wall seating, G-01); soft, natural materials; natural light; non-institutional aesthetics
- Panic exit provisions: all spaces where MH users are anticipated should have clearly visible, unobstructed exit routes; minimum two exit routes visible from primary seating areas
- Non-clinical aesthetic principle: accessible and MH-supportive design should be indistinguishable from high-quality mainstream design
- Sensory control: individual control over lighting, acoustic environment, and temperature where possible (H-02)
- Escape/breakout spaces accessible from primary circulation without requiring interaction (D-05)

*OT Framework: Person-Environment-Occupation (PEO) Model; trauma-informed care frameworks.*

*Evidence Base: Champagne (2011); Huckshorn (2004); Ulrich et al. (2008).*

---

### §2.9 PAIN — Chronic Pain and Fibromyalgia

**Definition**

Chronic pain conditions including fibromyalgia, complex regional pain syndrome, migraine, and chronic musculoskeletal pain.

**Core environmental needs**

- Hypersensitivity to tactile stimulation: smooth, consistent flooring preferred; avoid rough surfaces, vibration, and texture contrast at unexpected locations
- Allodynia provisions: cushioned seating at all rest points; heated floor options in residential settings
- Vibration isolation: floating plant rooms (HVAC, pumps, lifts) where fibromyalgia users are anticipated
- Rest seating: cushioned surfaces throughout; avoid hard plastic, metal, or rigid seating; armrests essential for lowering to seat and rising
- Cold floor surfaces: specify heated floor or seating with footrest at all primary seating areas

**Distinct from NEU:** PAIN conditions produce environmental sensitivity without the cognitive, balance, or fatigue profile of neurological conditions. See Part 8, §8.4.4 for temperature conflict between NEU (Uhthoff's — cool environments) and PAIN (warmth preference).

*OT Framework: Person-Environment-Occupation (PEO) Model.*

*Evidence Base: Desmeules et al. (2003); Staud (2011).*

---

### §2.10 DBL — DeafBlind

**Definition**

Combined vision and hearing loss, making both visual alerts and audio announcements inaccessible or insufficient. DeafBlind design requires a tactile-first approach: all information and navigation must be accessible through touch alone.

**Core environmental needs**

- Tactile-first: tactile information is primary; visual and audio provide redundancy only
- Vibrotactile alerting: personal pager alerting for all emergency and communication functions
- Consistent spatial layout: unexpected changes are not navigable by tactile methods
- Scent cues: olfactory orientation markers (consistent diffuser scent at key decision points) can supplement tactile navigation
- Interpreter seating proximity: DeafBlind communication frequently requires an interpreter seated adjacent; reserved zone of ≥2 seats (user + interpreter) at end of rows in all public assembly spaces; 1200 × 1200 mm clear floor zone per position
- Tactile Information System: embossed or thermoform tactile maps at all major decision points; Braille at all decision points; tactile floor routes connecting entrance → reception → key destinations → lift → escape route; updated every 3 years minimum or upon layout change

**Distinct from VIS:** VIS users have impairment in one sensory channel; redundant audio information remains accessible. DBL users have impairment in both channels; tactile-first provision is non-negotiable.

**Distinct from DEAF:** DEAF users have impairment in one sensory channel; redundant visual information remains accessible. DBL users have impairment in both channels; tactile-first provision is non-negotiable.

*OT Framework: Compensatory Frame of Reference; tactile-first design principle.*

*Evidence Base: DbI Nordic programme; Sense UK (2022); ISO 23599:2019.*

---

### §2.11 OFS — Orthostatic \& Fatigue Spectrum

The Orthostatic \& Fatigue Spectrum (OFS) encompasses three clinically distinct but frequently co-occurring conditions that share a common built environment design identity: post-exertional malaise, orthostatic intolerance, and multi-system environmental sensitivity.

**Definition**

| Sub-code | Condition | Core clinical mechanism |
| --- | --- | --- |
| OFS/ME | ME/CFS | Post-exertional malaise (PEM): symptom worsening following any exertion with delayed onset (12–48 hours). Orthostatic intolerance as diagnostic criterion. |
| OFS/POTS | POTS — Postural Orthostatic Tachycardia Syndrome | Pathological heart rate elevation on assuming upright posture; triggers include standing and exertion. Primary symptom: inability to sustain upright posture without tachycardia and pre-syncope. |
| OFS/MCAS | MCAS — Mast Cell Activation Syndrome | Mast cell degranulation triggered by chemical, thermal, and sensory stimuli; systemic reactions including cardiovascular, respiratory, and neurological symptoms. |

Prevalence: ME/CFS approximately 17 million globally (NIHR, 2023), substantially expanded by post-COVID populations. POTS estimated 0.2–1% general population, substantially higher post-COVID. MCAS population prevalence is uncertain — estimates range from under 1% in population-based studies to higher figures in selected clinical cohorts (Molderings et al., 2011 — specialist referral cohort; the 17% figure from this study should not be used as a general population prevalence estimate). OFS as a combined category is a mainstream design consideration for any building serving a general public.

**Core environmental needs**

- E-10: Rest seating on all circulation routes, ≤20 m interval, cushioned, armrests required
- F-04: Air quality provisions — MERV 13+ filtration, low-VOC specification, thermal stability
- F-05: Seated-task design — all primary occupational tasks achievable without sustained standing
- E-03: Ramp gradient ≤1:20
- E-04: Accessible parking ≤50 m to entry
- F-01: Sensory gradient from entry to primary space
- F-02: Fragrance-free zones extended to all OFS-occupied routes and spaces
- A-16: Sensory room / rest space with reclining option

**Distinct from MOB:** OFS users may have no mobility impairment. The access barrier is exertion, not physical obstruction. A fully MOB-accessible step-free route may still impose an unacceptable exertion cost for an OFS user if it requires sustained standing or exceeds 20 m without rest seating.

*OT Framework: Ecology of Human Performance (EHP) — energy conservation model.*

*Evidence Base: NINDS (2024); NIHR (2023); Patient Led Research Collaborative (2024); Blitshteyn \& Whitelaw (2021); Molderings et al. (2011).*

---

### §2.12 IntD — Intellectual Disability

*(Category under development)*

**Definition**

Intellectual disability (IntD) is characterised by significant limitations in intellectual functioning and adaptive behaviour, originating during the developmental period. It encompasses a wide range of presentations, from mild to profound, and frequently co-occurs with other disability categories (MOB, NDV, NEU, DEM).

**Special Considerations — IntD**

This category is a recognised gap in v9.0. A full Part II category with OT evidence basis, environmental design specifications, and Part III co-occurrence guidance is under development for v9.0.

**Core environmental needs**

Interim provisions until the full category is available:

- Wayfinding and navigation: apply DEM wayfinding provisions (D-01 loop plan; D-03 toilet visibility; D-04 wayfinding continuity; D-07 no blind corners) as the strongest available evidence base for cognitive accessibility
- Signage: apply pictogram + word signage (D-08) throughout; add Easy Read format for all public-facing written information
- Sensory environment: apply NDV sensory gradient provisions (F-01, F-02, F-03) where sensory processing differences are present
- Support worker access: ensure all spaces are accessible for two people side-by-side (MOB turning circle provisions; 1500 mm corridor minimum) to support users who require physical assistance

**Known evidence gaps:** Dedicated OT intervention research in built environments for intellectual disability is limited in the English-language literature. Multilingual research (German: Bundesvereinigung Lebenshilfe; Dutch: Vilans; Spanish: Plena inclusión) is in progress for the next edition. Practitioners should treat DEM and NDV provisions as the strongest available proxies until IntD-specific evidence is incorporated.

*OT Framework: pending IntD-specific evidence review. See Part 8 §8.4 for conflict resolutions applicable to IntD co-occurring with DEM and NDV populations.*

*Evidence Base: [PLACEHOLDER — IntD-specific built environment evidence review pending. Citation-verifier pass required before v9.0 publication.]*
## Part 3: Designing for Multiple Disability Categories

*Accessible Built Environments — Utopian

Best Practice Guidebook v9.0*

### §3.1 Introduction and Rationale

Disability is not experienced in isolation. Epidemiological data consistently show that more than 55% of disabled adults have two or more disability types simultaneously (WHO Global Disability Report, 2011; Australian Institute of Health and Welfare, 2022). The Code-as-Floor Principle (§1.2) applies to single-category provisions --- but for persons with multiple disability categories, even beyond-code single-category provisions may be systematically inadequate. Three failure modes are documented where single-category provisions are applied to multiple-category users: - Provisions for one category create barriers for another (e.g., tactile flooring for VIS creating vibration pain triggers for PAIN users; see Part 8 for conflict resolution). - Single-category specifications are calibrated to that category's OT evidence without accounting for the compound functional burden of co-occurring conditions. - Design briefs treat disability categories as discrete user groups, failing to design for the majority who present with multiple categories.

### §3.2 Theoretical Framework

#### §3.2.1 ICF and Intersectional Disability

The ICF (WHO, 2001) framework treats disability as arising from the interaction between health conditions, body functions and structures, and environmental and personal factors. Multiple co-occurring health conditions produce compounded environmental interaction — the same environmental barrier affects multiple functional domains simultaneously. A staircase is a mobility barrier for MOB; the effort of navigating around it is a fatigue trigger for CFS/ME; the cognitive demand of the reroute is an additional load for DEM. Single-category design addresses one of these interactions; multiple-category design addresses all three.

#### §3.2.2 Competence-Press Model at Compound Strain

Lawton \& Nahemow's (1973) Competence-Press Model holds that adaptive behaviour occurs in a zone of moderate environmental press relative to individual competence. For multiple-category users, the competence level relevant to environmental press is the lowest (most constrained) functional domain across all co-occurring categories. A person with MOB + CFS/ME has the physical mobility competence of the MOB category and the energy expenditure ceiling of the CFS/ME category. Environmental press that is appropriate for MOB alone may exceed the compound competence ceiling of MOB + CFS/ME. Design for multiple disability categories requires assessment of compound press against compound competence.

### §3.3 Co-occurrence Matrix

The following matrix shows documented co-occurrence frequency between disability category pairs.

**Ratings:** HIGH = frequently documented co-occurrence with direct design implications; MEDIUM = moderately documented; LOW = less common or indirect. *BAR provisions relocated: see Supplementary Volume "Designing for Different Body Sizes," Section IV. BAR column retained for cross-reference continuity (Option B interim).*

|  | MOB | VIS | NEU | DEM | NDV | MH | PAIN | BAR | UPL | DBL | OFS |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| **MOB** | — | MED | HIGH | HIGH | LOW | MED | HIGH | HIGH | HIGH | LOW | HIGH | | **VIS** | MED | — | MED | MED | MED | MED | LOW | LOW | LOW | HIGH | LOW | | **NEU** | HIGH | MED | — | MED | HIGH | HIGH | HIGH | MED | MED | LOW | HIGH | | **DEM** | HIGH | MED | MED | — | LOW | MED | MED | LOW | LOW | LOW | HIGH | | **NDV** | LOW | MED | HIGH | LOW | — | HIGH | MED | LOW | LOW | LOW | MED | | **MH** | MED | MED | HIGH | MED | HIGH | — | HIGH | MED | LOW | LOW | HIGH | | **PAIN** | HIGH | LOW | HIGH | MED | MED | HIGH | — | MED | MED | LOW | HIGH | | **BAR** | HIGH | LOW | MED | LOW | LOW | MED | MED | — | LOW | LOW | MED | | **UPL** | HIGH | LOW | MED | LOW | LOW | LOW | MED | LOW | — | LOW | LOW | | **DBL** | LOW | HIGH | LOW | LOW | LOW | LOW | LOW | LOW | LOW | — | LOW | | **OFS** | HIGH | LOW | HIGH | HIGH | MED | HIGH | HIGH | MED | LOW | LOW | — |  |

### §3.4 Key Co-occurrence Design Guidance
#### MOB + OFS (including CFS/ME, POTS, MS)

Rest seating every ≤20 m (E-10); lift provision even under 3 storeys; automatic doors (E-11); level entry. Energy expenditure budget governs all circulation decisions. MS-specific: thermal management (≤18°C, no humidity accumulation); temporal accessibility provision on high-fatigue days.

#### MOB + DEM

Loop floor plan (D-01) is essential — dead-end corridors generate agitation and disorientation for DEM users navigating with a wheelchair. Toilet visibility from bed (D-03). All MOB turning radii and DEM wayfinding provisions apply simultaneously.

#### NEU + PAIN (MS + fibromyalgia)

Thermal conflict: Uhthoff's (MS, NEU) requires cool temperature ≤18°C; fibromyalgia (PAIN) requires warmth. Resolution: zoned thermal management — primary activity spaces at ≤18°C with individual supplemental heating available (warm throw, portable radiant panel). See Part 8, §8.4.4.

#### NDV + MH (AUT + PTSD)

Sensory-retreat provision at ground-floor level (no lift required for crisis access); acoustic isolation STC ≥35; individual environmental control; defensible seating with back-to-wall; no exposed structural services overhead.

#### VIS + DBL

DeafBlind users with residual vision require high luminance contrast (LRV ≥50 at critical junctions, exceeding standard VIS ≥30 specification); tactile floor indicators; vibrotactile fire alerting; Braille at all key signs with tactile indicator approaching sign from walking surface.

#### DEM + OFS

Both categories require toilet proximity — DEM for cognitive navigation; OFS/POTS for orthostatic intolerance (sudden urgency, limited standing time). Toilet visible and accessible without more than 20 m travel from any primary occupied space.

#### MOB + NEU (Mobility and Neurological/ABI)

Common presentation: MS (MOB + NEU); stroke (MOB + NEU + DEM); TBI (MOB + NEU). Design must accommodate both the physical access layer and the neurological layer simultaneously. Physical provisions (turning circles, step-free, grab bars) are necessary but not sufficient --- without acoustic and lighting management, a physically accessible space may remain sensorially hostile for a NEU user with sensory hypersensitivity or post-concussion photophobia. Key items: D-01 (loop plan, reduces navigation cognitive load for ABI); B-09 (glare control — critical for PCS); A-13 (no sound masking); E-10 (rest seating, essential for MS fatigue). Temporal accessibility (good days/bad days) governs design: the space must remain accessible at low functional capacity.

#### MOB + PAIN (Mobility and Chronic Pain)

Common presentation: MS with fibromyalgia; arthritis (MOB + PAIN); rheumatoid arthritis (MOB + PAIN + UPL). Physical access provisions (grab bars, turning space) must be specified without creating vibration or cold-contact triggers. Key conflicts: grab bar surface temperature (cold metal triggers PAIN allodynia — specify insulated or coated grab bars per G-03); flooring vibration from textured tactile indicators (E-09) may trigger PAIN — use recessed indicators or acoustic vinyl alternative where PAIN is the primary population. Thermal management critical: PAIN users require warmth; MS users may require cool (see Part 8 §8.4.4 for resolution protocol).

#### NEU + MH (Neurological/ABI and Mental Health/PTSD)

Common presentation: TBI with PTSD (documented in veteran populations);

ABI with depression and anxiety. Both categories require: safe retreat space (A-16 sensory room); individual environmental control (H-02); acoustic isolation from high-stimulation environments (A-03, A-14); defensible seating (F-01). MH adds: no enclosed spaces without visible exits; no overhead exposed services (threat-activation trigger in PTSD); natural light access. NEU adds: no sound masking (A-13); glare suppression (B-09). The intersection is a low-stimulation, controllable environment with visible exits and natural light.

#### NEU + OFS (Neurological/ABI and Orthostatic/Fatigue Spectrum)

Common presentation: MS (NEU + OFS); post-COVID with both cognitive and fatigue symptoms. This pairing produces compound exertion budgeting: the OFS post-exertional malaise threshold constrains total activity volume; the NEU cognitive fatigue threshold constrains cognitive task duration. Design implication: rest seating with back support (not just perching options) at ≤15 m on primary routes (more frequent than the ≤20 m standard); seated workstation options for all primary tasks (F-05); access to all building functions without sustained standing or aerobic exertion (E-10 extended frequency). Thermal management for MS overlap: ≤18°C in primary activity spaces.

#### MH + PAIN (Mental Health/PTSD and Chronic Pain)

Common presentation: fibromyalgia with depression (PAIN + MH); chronic pain syndrome with PTSD. Both populations benefit from: individual thermal and acoustic control (H-02); access to natural light (biophilic provisions); fragrance-free environments (F-02) — pain amplification is worsened by chemical sensitivity. PAIN requires warmth; MH requires controllability. Resolution: individual radiant heating available at seated workstations, with cool ambient air as default. Design must not create environments that amplify pain perception (hard surfaces, cold materials, high acoustic reverberation all documented as pain-amplifying environmental factors).

#### MH + OFS (Mental Health/PTSD and Orthostatic/Fatigue Spectrum)

Common presentation: PTSD with ME/CFS; anxiety disorder with POTS (documented in post-COVID populations). Both categories require: low exertion access (E-10 rest seating, step-free throughout); individual environmental control (H-02); and access to retreat without navigating high-stimulation spaces. MH adds: visibility of exits; choice of open or enclosed seating; no overhead surveillance or enclosed approaches to spaces. OFS adds: no sustained standing requirements; no routes requiring aerobic exertion. The design intersection is a low-exertion, controllable environment with spatial choice.
# Volume II: Application Manual and Engineering

## How to Use This Volume

Volume 2 translates the 71-item technique library into application tools for design practice. It is organised in five parts, followed by supplementary resources.

## Population Codes Reference

| Code | Population | Primary Design Concerns |
| --- | --- | --- |
| MOB | Mobility and strength | Step-free, turning circles, reach ranges, transfer space |
| VIS | Vision (low vision to blind) | Contrast, tactile wayfinding, lighting levels, glare |
| DEAF | Deaf and hard of hearing; DeafBlind (communication systems).** 
*Note: Deaf with a capital D refers to members of the Deaf cultural community for whom signed language is a primary language. Design provisions must support visual communication and signed language use — not only audiological accommodation. See NAD (2023) position statement.* | Visual alarms, loop systems, captioning, visual intercom; sightlines for signed language; DeafSpace design principles |
| NEU | Neurological and acquired brain injury | Fatigue, sensory processing, motor control variability |
| DEM | Dementia | Wayfinding, orientation, sensory calm, positive stimulation |
| NDV | Neurodivergent (autism, ADHD, sensory processing difference) | Acoustic and sensory control, predictability, choice |
| NDV/MH | Mental health, PTSD, trauma | Safety, control, enclosure options, sensory de-escalation |
| PAIN | Chronic pain and fibromyalgia | Thermal stability, vibration, touch sensitivity, rest |
| BAR | Large Body Size (Relocated → Supp Vol IV) | Structural capacity, turning circles, furniture ratings |
| OFS | Orthostatic and fatigue spectrum (POTS, CFS/ME, MS fatigue) | Seating, thermal stability, exertion management |
| DBL | DeafBlind | Tactile communication, route predictability, combined VIS+DEAF provisions |
| UPL | Upper limb and amputation (sub-code of MOB) | Hardware operability, reach, grip-free operation |
*Note: Intellectual disability (IntD) is a recognised gap in this edition. Category under development for v9.0. Practitioners should apply DEM wayfinding provisions as interim guidance for users with significant cognitive disability.* — |

## Entry Path I: By Design Stage

What must I do at each stage that cannot be undone later?

| Design Stage | Critical Decisions — Cannot be Retrofitted | Items |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **SD — Schematic Design** | Lift shaft location and size | E-01 |
|  | Corridor widths ≥1200 mm on all primary routes | E-08 |

## NOT-RETROFITTABLE Items — Consolidated Table

The following items cannot be incorporated after structural design is complete without major cost and structural intervention. All must be confirmed on named drawings at the design stage indicated.

| Item | Title | Stage | Consequence of omission | Must appear on |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| E-01 | Accessible lift (platform or full passenger) | SD | Permanent level-change barrier | Floor plans; structural drawings |
| D-03 | Toilet visibility from living/bedroom (DEM) | SD | Permanent wayfinding barrier for DEM users | Floor plan |
| D-01 | Loop floor plan (no dead ends) | SD | Permanent cognitive disorientation risk for DEM | Floor plan |
| E-06 | Level entry (zero step threshold) | SD | Permanent physical access barrier | Site plan; section |
| I-04 | Accessible bathroom (zero-threshold shower, drainage) | SD | Permanent drainage and floor structural barrier | Section; structural drawings |
| A-08 | HVAC noise control (plant room location) | SD | Permanent mechanical noise intrusion | Mechanical plans; acoustic strategy |
| A-09 | HVAC vibration isolation (floating plant room) | SD | Permanent vibration transmission | Structural/mechanical drawings |
| J-04 (BAR → Supp Vol IV) | large body size wet room structural floor (BAR → Supp Vol IV) | SD | Permanent structural inadequacy | Structural drawings (SE confirmation) |
| I-03 | Grab bar blocking (structural) | CD | Retrofit cost ×75 | Structural/blocking drawings |
| R-STA-02 | Stair structural channel for future stairlift | CD | Retrofit cost ×15–30 | Structural drawings | — |
