## 9.5 Structural Engineer Coordination

Structural engineering operates under a distinct scope from acoustic, mechanical, and electrical engineering. The items below are collected separately because structural accessibility provisions are among the highest-cost items to retrofit, are frequently missing from SE briefs, and must be confirmed on structural drawings — not only in the specification text. Structural drawings are the confirmation document; a specification entry without a corresponding structural drawing is not a structural commitment.

| Item | SE Requirement | Stage | Drawing Confirmation | If Missed |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| E-01 | Lift shaft: minimum internal clear dimensions per EN 81-70; designed to accommodate future full-passenger upgrade where platform lift is the interim measure | SD | Floor plans; structural drawings ⚠ | ×50+ |
| E-06 | Threshold structural detail: level threshold achievable at all principal entries; drainage slab recess coordinated with ME | SD | Section drawing ⚠ | Not retrofittable |
| E-12 | Refuge enclosure: fire-rated partition structural requirements at each stair core; evacuation lift shaft structural co-location | SD | Structural drawings ⚠ | Major structural intervention |
| A-09 | Floating plant room: floating concrete slab on spring isolators; structural separation from occupied floors; slab mass and isolator specification per ME requirements | SD | Structural drawings ⚠ | Not retrofittable |
| B-09 | Structural openings for clerestory, rooflight, or light well: lintel sizing, weatherproofing interface | SD | Structural drawings ⚠ | ×10 per opening |
| G-03 | Grab bar blocking: all bathrooms, shower rooms, WCs, and any space where future grab bar installation is anticipated. Rated load ≥1.5 kN point load per ISO 11199. Blocking extends 300 mm beyond anticipated grab bar positions on all four sides. Photographic record required before wall lining is applied. | CD | Structural/blocking drawings ⚠ | ×75 |
| I-04 | Zero-threshold shower floor recess: structural slab recess for drainage channel; recess depth coordinated with ME drainage design | SD | Structural/section drawings ⚠ | ×20–40 |
| I-05 | Ceiling hoist tracking: structural blocking or dedicated track support in all designated bedrooms and bathrooms; track layout confirmed with architect; minimum 800 kg dynamic load rating | CD | Structural drawings ⚠ | ×20–40 |

**SE brief standard clause:**
> *All structural provisions for accessibility are primary structural design requirements, not secondary modifications. DAR (Design for Adaptable Readiness) provisions are to be incorporated at the earliest stage. Published retrofit cost multipliers for structural accessibility provisions: grab bar blocking ×75; ceiling hoist tracking ×20–40; lift shaft ×50+ (Rick Hansen Foundation 2023; TERRAGON/DStGB 2017). These figures are to be cited in any value engineering discussion where structural accessibility provisions are challenged. No structural accessibility provision is to be removed from the design without the written agreement of the lead architect.*
## Part 10: Working with Specialist Consultants

## 10.0 Overview

Part 10 addresses the architect's coordination role with specialist consultants whose input is required for accessible design that cannot be delivered through engineering briefs alone. The engineering disciplines covered in Part 9 — acoustic, mechanical, electrical, and structural — operate on measurable physical systems. The consultants addressed here operate on the relationship between people and environments: a different domain, requiring a different coordination logic.

The central specialist is the occupational therapist. OT practice is the primary evidence base for this entire guidebook (Volume 1, §1.4 Evidence Hierarchy — Tier 1). An architect working with this guidebook is, in effect, working from a distillation of OT evidence. When the individual client or user population is known, that distillation must be supplemented by direct OT involvement.

Other specialist consultants addressed in this Part — dementia design specialists, DeafSpace consultants, sensory design specialists, and accessibility auditors — each operate on an evidence base with a specific population scope. The appointment triggers and scope of services for each are defined below.

**What this Part does not cover.** Hearing loop specialists and audiologists are addressed in Part 9 §9.1 as acoustic engineering sub-specialists. Post-occupancy evaluation protocols are addressed in Part 13. Legal compliance review by access consultants operating under code frameworks is addressed in Appendix A (Global Standards Catalogue). Fire engineers' involvement in emergency evacuation for disabled occupants is addressed in Appendix D.

---

## 9.1 The Three-Tier Hierarchy and Specialist Consultant Appointment

The Three-Tier Design Hierarchy (Volume 1, §1.6.3) governs when specialist consultant input is required and what its scope must be.

| Tier | Standard | User Knowledge | Specialist Requirement |
|---|---|---|---|
| Tier 0 | Universal Design / Code Compliance | Anonymous | None required. Design from this guidebook's library items at their stated medians. |
| Tier 1 | Population-Informed Inclusive Design | Identified population (not individual) | Required where the building's primary population includes NDV, DEM, NEU, OFS, or DBL, or where two or more primary populations are present. Required where co-occurrence patterns produce conflicts that population-level medians cannot resolve. Recommended for all other Tier 1 projects. |
| Tier 2 | Person-Specific Inclusive Design | Named individual | OT assessment mandatory. Design cannot proceed to Tier 2 specification without a completed functional capacity assessment for the named person. Co-design process required. |

DAR (Design for Adaptable Readiness) is mandatory at all three tiers regardless of specialist involvement. DAR provisions, once confirmed in the structural and engineering briefs, do not require specialist sign-off — they are standard construction-stage provisions documented in Part 10.

The boundary between tiers is a project decision, not an option. A residential commission for a named client with a known disability is Tier 2 by definition. A dementia care facility serving an identified population is Tier 1. A speculative apartment building where no user population can be characterised is Tier 0. Misclassifying a project at a lower tier than the evidence warrants is a design failure, not a cost saving.

---

## 9.2 Occupational Therapist

### 9.2.1 Role and Scope

Occupational therapists are the trained professionals whose discipline directly addresses the person-environment-occupation relationship. OT practice is built on the assessment of how a specific person performs specific activities in specific environments — and what environmental modifications would enable greater occupational performance and independence. This is precisely the evidence domain this guidebook draws on.

An OT engaged in a building project is not providing an accessibility audit in the compliance sense. The OT's role is to bring functional capacity evidence — either for a named individual (Tier 2) or for a defined population (Tier 1) — into the design process in a form that the architect can translate into spatial and technical specifications.

The OT does not produce drawings or engineering specifications. The OT produces clinical knowledge that the architect must translate. This translation responsibility sits with the architect and is the reason this guidebook exists.

### 9.2.2 Appointment Triggers

Appoint an OT when any of the following conditions apply:

- **Tier 2 project** — a named individual is the primary occupant or user. Mandatory. OT assessment is the mechanism by which Tier 1 specification ranges are resolved to person-specific values. Without OT assessment, Tier 2 design is not achievable; median Tier 1 values must be used instead, with explicit documentation that individual assessment has not been completed.
- **Residential adaptations to an occupied dwelling** — where a current occupant has a disability and the design brief is to improve that person's independence in their own home. OT referral may precede architectural involvement; the OT assessment report is the primary brief document.
- **Tier 1 project with NDV, DEM, NEU, OFS, or DBL as a primary population** — these populations require environmental calibration beyond the standard design library medians. Sensory zoning (F-01 through F-07), acoustic environment, and individual control provisions (H-01, H-02) require OT review of the proposed environment against population-specific functional profiles before technical design is finalised.
- **Healthcare, supported housing, or specialist education environments** — where the gap between code compliance and genuine occupational independence is at its greatest, and where OT involvement is standard professional practice in most jurisdictions.
- **Post-occupancy evaluation** — where the building has been completed and occupied, and the design team or building owner requires evidence of whether the environment is meeting its accessibility intent. See Part 13.

### 9.2.3 Scope of Services by Design Stage

The following table sets out the minimum scope of OT involvement at each design stage where appointment has been triggered. The scope expands at Tier 2 and contracts at Tier 1; the minimum for each tier is stated.

| Stage | Tier 1 Minimum OT Scope | Tier 2 Minimum OT Scope |
|---|---|---|
| Brief / Pre-Design | Review population brief. Confirm which population codes are primary and which are secondary. Flag any population-specific provisions that fall outside the standard library medians. Identify Tier 2 triggers if any. | Complete functional capacity assessment of named individual. Produce assessment report structured for architectural use (see VI.2.4). Confirm DAR provisions required for anticipated functional trajectory. |
| Schematic Design | Review room adjacency diagram against sensory gradient strategy (F-01). Confirm NOT-RETROFITTABLE items relevant to the population are on the schematic floor plan. Flag any spatial layout decisions that will compromise occupational performance for the identified population. | Review schematic floor plan against assessment report. Confirm toilet position (D-03), grab bar blocking zones (G-03), hoist track zones (I-05), and circulation widths (E-08). Issue schematic-stage specification inputs to architect. Co-design session with named occupant. |
| Design Development | Review item specifications against population profiles. Confirm sensory gradient is deliverable with proposed layout. Review H-01 and H-02 control positions against functional reach envelopes. Confirm accessible bathroom layout (I-04) against population-specific transfer requirements. | Review item specifications against individual assessment. Confirm all Tier 1 ranges are resolved to person-specific values. Co-design session with named occupant on layout, control positions, and assistive technology interface. Issue Tier 2 specification schedule to architect. |
| Construction Documents | Review specification for consistency with OT scope of services outputs. Flag any changes from Design Development that affect occupational performance. | Review drawings for consistency with Tier 2 specification schedule. Confirm grab bar positions on blocking drawing. Confirm hoist track layout. Issue construction-stage compliance check. |
| Ready for Occupancy | Attend ready for occupancy walkthrough. Assess whether completed environment meets the OT-specified outcomes. Issue ready for occupancy assessment report. | Ready for occupancy assessment with named occupant present where possible. Assess occupational performance in completed environment. Issue assessment report identifying any outstanding modifications required. |
| Post-Occupancy | Contribute to POE at 6 and 12 months. Assess occupational performance outcomes against design intent. Identify any residual barriers for gap register. | POE with named occupant. Assess change in occupational independence since occupation. Document outcomes. Recommend any further adaptations. |

### 9.2.4 OT Assessment Report — Architectural Brief Format

For Tier 2 projects, the OT assessment report is the primary brief document from which architectural specifications are derived. To be usable for this purpose, the report must be structured to include:

- Functional capacity profile by domain (mobility, reach, grip, visual, cognitive, sensory processing, communication)
- Functional variation across time (good-day/bad-day range for fluctuating conditions)
- Current assistive technology in use and anticipated future technology
- Priority occupations (the activities that matter most to the individual and that the environment must support)
- Specific environmental barriers in the current environment (for adaptation projects)
- Specification inputs in architectural terms: turning circle requirement; reach envelope; transfer approach (side, angled); grab bar positions; threshold and gradient requirements; lighting, acoustic, and thermal preferences
- DAR recommendations: provisions for anticipated functional trajectory over building lifespan

An OT report that does not include specification inputs in architectural terms cannot be used directly as a design brief. Where a report is received without this structure, the architect should request a supplementary architectural brief session with the OT before schematic design commences.

### 9.2.5 Co-Design and CRPD Article 4.3

OT assessment does not fulfil the co-design obligation in CRPD Article 4.3. The Convention requires States Parties to actively involve disabled people in the development of accessibility standards and measures. This obligation extends to individual building projects where a named person is the occupant: the person's own preferences, priorities, and choices are required inputs to the design alongside OT clinical findings.

Co-design is not user consultation at the end of a design process. It is structured involvement of the disabled person — and where relevant, their family or support network — in design decisions throughout the process. At minimum, co-design sessions are to be held at schematic design stage and at developed design stage. Where the individual's communication needs require adapted participation formats, the OT advises on appropriate process design.

Lived experience of the relevant condition(s) is also a form of evidence. Where the individual client is the primary co-designer, this is straightforward. Where the project serves a population rather than a named individual (Tier 1), the design team is to engage disabled people with lived experience of the relevant condition(s) in design review. The Kelsey IDS co-design methodology, the Gallaudet DeafSpace participatory design process, and Motionspot's neuroinclusive design protocols are established frameworks for population-level co-design; see Volume 1, §1.5 (Evidence Hierarchy — Co-primary Tier 1).

### 9.2.6 Relationship to this Guidebook

This guidebook is a distillation of OT evidence into architectural specification form. Its design library items (Part 7) are derived from Tier 1 sources — primarily OT clinical research and lived experience evidence — and expressed as population-informed ranges calibrated to functional capacity distributions.

An architect using this guidebook without OT involvement is working at Tier 1 using population medians. This is appropriate for straightforward Tier 1 projects — a single primary population, low co-occurrence complexity, and building types where the standard library medians are well-calibrated to the anticipated user profile. It is not appropriate where multiple primary populations are present, where co-occurrence patterns produce irresolvable conflicts under the standard library alone, or where the populations involved (NDV, DEM, NEU, OFS, DBL) require environmental calibration beyond what population-level medians can reliably deliver. In those circumstances, OT or specialist consultant involvement at Tier 1 is the appropriate standard, not an elective enhancement. It is never appropriate for Tier 2 projects, and it does not replace OT involvement where the appointment triggers in §10.2.2 are met.

Where OT assessment findings conflict with the specification ranges in Part 7 of this guidebook, the OT assessment governs for the individual concerned. The ranges in Part 7 define the population evidence envelope; the individual's functional capacity determines where within that envelope the design should land. Departures from the stated range require documented clinical justification from the OT.

---

## 9.3 Dementia Design Specialist

### 9.3.1 Role

A dementia design specialist applies the environmental research base specific to dementia — principally the DSDC (Dementia Services Development Centre, University of Stirling) evidence body — to building design and assessment. The DSDC EADDAT tool (Environments for Ageing and Dementia Design Assessment Tool, 2022) provides the primary audit and design framework; see Volume 1, §1.5 (Evidence Hierarchy) and the 17-Jurisdiction Research Compendium §B3 for evidence classification.

A dementia design specialist is not a substitute for an OT where individual Tier 2 assessment is required. The two roles are complementary: the dementia design specialist brings environmental design expertise calibrated to the dementia population; the OT brings functional capacity assessment of the individual.

### 9.3.2 Appointment Triggers

Appoint a dementia design specialist where:

- DEM is a primary population code for the building
- The project is a dementia care facility, memory care unit, specialist housing, or any environment where the primary users include people with dementia
- The project involves spatial decisions with the highest DEM-specific stakes: floor plan topology (D-01), toilet visibility from primary living space (D-03), wayfinding landmark strategy (D-04), and colour strategy (C-02, C-05, C-06)

### 9.3.3 Scope

The dementia design specialist reviews the design against the DSDC EADDAT framework and the DEM-specific items in Part 7 of this guidebook (Category C, D, and F items where DEM is a primary population). The specialist's primary contribution is at schematic design stage — confirming the spatial topology and adjacency decisions that are NOT-RETROFITTABLE. At developed design stage, the specialist reviews colour strategy, signage system, and sensory environment against the DEM evidence base.

**Relationship to this guidebook:** DEM item specifications in Part 7 (D-01, D-03, D-04, C-02, C-05) are derived from DSDC evidence. A dementia design specialist using the DSDC framework will be working from the same evidence base. Where findings diverge, the specific DSDC EADDAT assessment for the project environment governs over the guidebook's general population-level specifications.

---

## 9.4 DeafSpace / Deaf Design Consultant

### 9.4.1 Role

A DeafSpace or Deaf design consultant applies the Gallaudet University DeafSpace design principles — a catalogue of 100+ spatial and material patterns developed through participatory design with Deaf communities — to building projects where Deaf or hard of hearing users are the primary population. See Volume 1, §1.5 (Evidence Hierarchy) and the 17-Jurisdiction Research Compendium §B2 for evidence classification.

The DeafSpace framework addresses five design categories: space and proximity (sightlines for signed language); sensory reach (visual communication range); mobility and proximity (how spatial sequences affect Deaf social interaction); light and colour (conditions for visual communication); acoustics (for hearing device users and those with residual hearing). Several of these — particularly sightline and spatial proximity decisions — fall outside the standard accessible design library and require specialist input.

### 9.4.2 Appointment Triggers

Appoint a DeafSpace or Deaf design consultant where:

- DEAF is a primary population code and the project involves significant social, educational, or civic function — spaces where visual communication and signed language use are primary modes of interaction
- The project is a Deaf school, Deaf community facility, or any environment designed primarily for Deaf users
- Sightline strategy and spatial proximity design are primary design decisions (common in open-plan offices, classrooms, and communal residential spaces)

### 9.4.3 Scope

The DeafSpace consultant reviews sightline strategy, spatial sequence, lighting quality for visual communication, and the design library items most relevant to DEAF users (A-10, A-11, H-03, H-04, B-10). The consultant's primary contribution is at schematic design stage — confirming that the spatial topology supports visual communication — and at developed design stage, for lighting and surface finish decisions.

**Relationship to this guidebook:** The DEAF items in Part 7 address hearing loop infrastructure (A-10, A-11), visual fire alarm (B-10), and accessible intercom (H-04). These are the engineering-deliverable provisions. The spatial and proxemic provisions of DeafSpace design fall largely outside the Part 7 library and are the specialist's primary contribution.

---

## 9.5 Sensory Design Consultant

### 9.5.1 Role

A sensory design consultant applies population-specific sensory environment evidence — principally PAS 6463:2022 (BSI), the ASPECTSS Design Index (Mostafa), and the OT sensory processing literature — to buildings where NDV, NEU, or MH populations are primary users. The consultant's scope overlaps significantly with OT practice in sensory environments; in many projects, a suitably qualified OT with sensory processing expertise fulfils both roles.

### 9.5.2 Appointment Triggers

Appoint a sensory design consultant where:

- NDV (including NDV/AUT, NDV/ADHD, NDV/SENS) is a primary population code
- The project is a school, workplace, healthcare facility, or supported housing development with a significant neurodivergent population
- Sensory gradient strategy (F-01 through F-07) and the sensory room specification (A-16) are major design decisions requiring population-specific calibration beyond the standard library medians

### 9.5.3 Scope

The sensory design consultant reviews the sensory gradient strategy (F-01), sensory room specification (A-16), acoustic specification (Categories A), lighting specification (Category B), and material/finish choices (Category C) against the NDV/NEU population evidence base. The consultant's primary contribution is at schematic design stage — confirming zone placement and adjacency — and at developed design stage, for material and finish specification.

**Relationship to this guidebook:** The F-category items in Part 7 provide population-level specifications for sensory zoning. The sensory design consultant applies these to the specific building layout and user population, confirms calibration, and identifies where project-specific conditions require deviation from standard medians.

---

## 9.6 Accessibility Auditor

### 9.6.1 Role

An accessibility auditor provides an independent assessment of a completed or near-completed building's compliance with applicable accessibility standards and with the design intent of the accessibility specification. The auditor's role is verification, not design — the auditor operates after design decisions have been made and construction has occurred or is near completion.

An accessibility audit in the compliance sense (code verification against statutory minimum) is distinct from a functional accessibility assessment of the kind an OT provides. The former asks: does this building meet the applicable standard? The latter asks: can a person with a specific disability use this building independently? Both questions are relevant; they are not the same question.

### 9.6.2 Appointment Triggers

Commission an accessibility audit where:

- The project requires independent verification of code compliance (many healthcare, education, and publicly funded projects require this)
- The design team has specified above-code accessibility provisions and requires verification that those provisions have been delivered as specified
- Post-occupancy: a building is the subject of an access complaint, a change-of-use application, or a significant refurbishment that requires current baseline assessment

### 9.6.3 Relationship to this Guidebook

An accessibility audit verifying compliance with this guidebook's provisions should reference the item specification library (Part 7) as the specification baseline, not only the statutory code. The audit checklist maps directly to the Part 7 item library. Where items are found to be non-compliant, findings are to be recorded against the item code for gap register entry.

The Rick Hansen Foundation Accessibility Certification (RHFAC) programme and the WELL Accessibility Rating provide structured audit frameworks aligned with above-code accessible design practice; see the 17-Jurisdiction Research Compendium §A3 (Canada) and §A1 (USA) respectively.

---

## 9.7 Coordination Protocol Across All Specialist Consultants

### 9.7.1 Sequencing

Specialist consultant inputs must feed the design process in the right sequence. The following sequence governs where multiple specialist consultants are engaged:

1. **OT assessment** (Tier 2) or **population brief confirmation** (Tier 1) — completed before schematic design commences. This is the input that calibrates all subsequent design decisions.
2. **Dementia design specialist / DeafSpace consultant / sensory design consultant** — engaged at schematic design stage. Their review of the spatial topology must occur before the schematic floor plan is signed off, since their primary concerns (toilet visibility, sightline geometry, zone placement) are NOT-RETROFITTABLE.
3. **Engineering consultants** (Part 9) — brief issued after specialist consultant schematic-stage inputs have been incorporated. The engineering brief reflects the spatial and performance decisions confirmed by specialist consultant review.
4. **OT developed design review** (Tier 2) — after engineering brief is issued and item specifications are drafted. The OT confirms that the technical specifications match the individual's functional requirements.
5. **Accessibility auditor** — commissioned at ready for occupancy or post-occupancy.

### 9.7.2 The Architect's Coordination Responsibility

The architect, as prime consultant, is responsible for ensuring that specialist consultant inputs are:

- Received at the right stage (not after the relevant design decision has been made)
- Translated from clinical or specialist language into architectural specification
- Communicated to other consultants whose scope is affected
- Documented in the project specification with traceability to the source assessment or review

Specialist consultant outputs are not self-executing. An OT assessment report that is received and filed without architectural translation has not been incorporated into the design. A dementia design specialist's review that is received after the schematic floor plan is signed off has arrived too late. The coordination responsibility is the architect's.

### 9.7.3 Documentation

The following documents are to be produced and maintained as part of the project record where specialist consultants are engaged:

| Document | Produced by | Timing | Filing requirement |
|---|---|---|---|
| OT functional capacity assessment report (Tier 2) | OT | Before SD | Project brief file |
| OT architectural brief (Tier 2) | OT (with architect) | Before SD | Project specification file |
| Tier 2 specification schedule | Architect (from OT brief) | DD | Specification file |
| Schematic-stage specialist review report(s) | Specialist consultant(s) | End of SD | Design review file |
| Co-design session records | Architect (facilitated) | SD and DD | Project record |
| OT developed design review (Tier 2) | OT | Mid-DD | Design review file |
| Ready for occupancy assessment | OT / auditor | RFO | Handover file; O&M manual |
| Post-occupancy evaluation | OT / specialist | 6 months and 12 months | POE register |

### 9.7.4 VE Protection — Specialist Consultant Scope

The following specialist consultant scopes are not to be reduced or removed from the project without written agreement of the lead architect:

- OT assessment (Tier 2 projects) — removing OT assessment from a Tier 2 project means the design reverts to Tier 1 medians; this must be documented and the client informed
- Dementia design specialist schematic review (DEM primary population) — the NOT-RETROFITTABLE schematic decisions (D-01, D-03) cannot be verified without this review; removing it creates unresolvable design risk at the stage where intervention costs are highest
- Accessibility audit at ready for occupancy — where required by the project brief or funding conditions, this is a contract deliverable and cannot be omitted

---

## 9.8 Disability Organisations and Lived Experience Input

Specialist consultant appointment and OT assessment are clinical and technical processes. They do not substitute for direct engagement with disabled people and disability organisations — a distinct form of knowledge with distinct validity.

Disability organisations — advocacy groups, community organisations, peer support networks, and user-led organisations — hold lived experience knowledge that clinical assessment cannot access: the knowledge of what it is like to navigate environments designed without adequate input from the people who use them. This knowledge is co-primary evidence under this guidebook's evidence hierarchy (Volume 1, §1.5 — Co-primary Tier 1).

For Tier 1 projects, engagement with the relevant disability community organisations is recommended at schematic design stage. For building types where disabled people are the primary user population (dementia care, Deaf schools, supported housing, neuroinclusive workplaces), this engagement should be structured and documented. The organisations listed in the 17-Jurisdiction Research Compendium §A1–A17 (jurisdiction-specific disability organisations) provide appropriate contact points by country.

For Tier 2 projects, the named individual and their chosen support network are the primary lived experience input. The co-design process described in §10.2.5 applies.

---

*Cross-reference: §1.4 (OT as Tier 1 evidence) · §1.5 (Evidence Hierarchy — Co-primary Tier 1) · §1.6.3 (Three-Tier Hierarchy) · Part 9 (Engineering coordination) · Part 11 (DAR provisions) · Part 13 (Post-Occupancy Evaluation) · 17-Jurisdiction Research Compendium §B2 (DeafSpace), §B3 (DSDC), §B5 (Sensory Zoning) · Appendix D (Emergency Evacuation — PEEP)*
# Volume III: DAR, Economics, Evidence and Appendices

All Part 7 item codes cited in Volume III use the v9.0 item codes: Category F = Sensory Zoning; Category G = Furniture/Interiors; Category H = Controls/Technology; Category I = Upper Limb; Category K = DeafBlind Environment Provisions. Bariatric provisions (formerly Category J) are in the Supplementary Volume, Supp. Part 4. See Part 7 for the full item specification library.

## Part 11: Design for Adaptable Readiness (DAR)

*Accessible Built Environments — Utopian Best Practice Guidebook v9.0*

*Volume 3*

### §11.0 Scope

Part 11 addresses homes and buildings that can adapt to changing functional needs without full reconstruction. The core argument is asymmetric and economic: the cost of not designing for adaptability is not zero. The most expensive accessible features are prohibitively expensive as retrofits but negligible at construction when planned at schematic design stage.

For the full international evidence base on new-build versus retrofit cost economics, including the macro cost ratio (0.5--1.3% new construction vs 10--40× retrofit), see Part 12. The table in §11.1 below provides the item-level cost multipliers; Part 12 §12.2 provides the macro context, and Part 12 §12.4 provides technique-level cost intelligence for all Part 7 items.

### §11.1 The DAR Cost Multiplier Framework

The following table presents the cost case for DAR provisions. All figures are approximate; see Part 12 §8.2 for the full international evidence base.

| Provision | At Construction | Retrofit Cost | Multiplier |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Grab bar blocking (structural) | £40 (CAD <$68) | £3,000 (CAD $680–$5,100) | \~75× |
| Ceiling hoist infrastructure | £25 (CAD <$43) | £3,000–£6,000 (CAD $5,100–$10,200) | \~150× |
| Through-floor lift shaft space | £2,000–£5,000 (CAD $3,400–$8,500) | £20,000–£50,000 (CAD $34,000–$85,000) | 10× |
| Wider bathroom (+1 additional m²) | £1,500 (CAD $1,360–$2,550) | £15,000–£40,000 (CAD $25,500–$68,000) | 10–25× |
| Level entry (no-step approach) | £2,000 (CAD $850–$3,400) | £5,000–£30,000 (CAD $8,500–$51,000) | 2.5–15× |
| Electrical conduit for stairlift | £500 (CAD $340–$850) | £2,000 + unit (CAD $1,360–$3,400 + unit) | 4×+ |
| DAR framing for wider door | £200/door (CAD $340/door) | £1,500–£5,000/door (CAD $2,550–$8,500/door) | 7–25× |
| Large body size floor loading (BAR → Supp. Part 4) floor loading (4.0 kN/m²) | £500 (CAD $340–$850) $340–$850) | £5,000–£20,000 (CAD $8,500–$34,000) | 10–40× | *Sources: CAN/ASC 2.8:2025 (current as of January 2026) cost analysis; Habinteg (2010) Lifetime Homes; RCOT Living Well by Design |

**Policy implication:** DAR provision in new construction is the highest-return accessible design investment available. Building codes that do not require DAR provisions impose these multiplied costs on ageing homeowners and disability communities.

### §11.2 CAN/ASC 2.8:2025 (current as of January 2026) DAR Core Provisions

Canada's CAN/ASC 2.8:2025 (current as of January 2026) specifies the following minimum DAR provisions for all new accessible-ready housing. Each provision is a 'rough-in' at construction enabling future accessible modification without structural demolition.

| Code | Provision | Standard Reference |
| --- | --- | --- |
| VII-DAR-01 | Entry: step-free approach; ≥950 mm structural opening; automatic door conduit; covered entry provision | Clause 7.2 |
| VII-DAR-02 | Ground floor / elevator-accessible floor: all essential rooms on one lift-accessible level OR through-floor lift shaft zone ≥900 × 1400 mm with structural provision | Clause 7.4 |
| VII-DAR-03 | Bathroom: bilateral grab bar blocking at 800–900 mm AFF; shower framed for future wet room; floor loading ≥135 kg; bathroom ≥1200 × 1800 mm | Clause 7.5 |
| VII-DAR-04 | Ceiling hoist: structural provision along transfer path (bedroom → bathroom); ceiling joists ≥135 kg point load per bracket at 600 mm spacing; electrical conduit to accessible panel | Clause 7.6 |
| VII-DAR-05 | Stairlift: stair clear width ≥850 mm; electrical conduit from bottom to top landing; structural blocking at head and foot | Clause 7.7 |
| VII-DAR-06 | Controls/technology: conduit to all major control points; smart home hub provision; voice-activated device electrical provision; WiFi AP conduit | Clause 7.8 |  |

### §11.3 Lifetime Homes 16 Criteria (UK, Habinteg 2010)

The UK Lifetime Homes standard specifies 16 design criteria. Key criteria with greatest accessibility impact:

| Criterion | Description | DAR cost argument |
| --- | --- | --- |
| 1 | Car parking: accessible or adaptable | 4× retrofit |
| 2 | Approach: level/easy gradient with cover | 10× retrofit |
| 3 | Entrance: level/flush threshold, ≥775 mm clear | 15× retrofit |
| 5 | Hallways: ≥1050 mm min clear | 20× retrofit |
| 8 | Through-floor lift: zone identified on plan | 25× retrofit |
| 11 | Bathroom: structural support for future grab bars | 10× retrofit |
| 12 | Bathroom: future wet room conversion provision | 10× retrofit |  |

### §11.4 Visitability: The 3-Criterion Baseline (USA, Concrete Change)

Visitability proposes a minimum 3-criterion standard for all new single-family and low-rise residential construction:

- At least one zero-step entrance
- Interior doors with ≥813 mm (32 inches) clear width on the main floor
- A toilet room on the main floor

Visitability adds <0.5% to construction cost (Maisel, 2006). It enables any person to visit the dwelling with dignity — without it, a wheelchair user cannot attend a social event in the home of a friend.

### §11.5 Aging in Place: OT Evidence Summary The systematic review of home modifications for aging in place (PMC,

2024; literature 2010--2024) identified the following highest-evidence home modifications, in order of evidence strength:

- **Bathroom modifications** (grab bars, shower seat, wet room): highest evidence; documented reduction in falls, ADL dependence, and care home admissions
- **Entry and threshold modifications** (level entry, ramp, automatic door): second-highest evidence; directly enable social participation
- **Lighting modifications** (motion-activated, night-light path, dimmable): high evidence for fall prevention; low cost; most commonly deferred until after a fall
- **Kitchen modifications** (lowered counters, pull-out storage, lever taps): high evidence for meal preparation independence
- **Environmental controls** (accessible thermostat, smart controls): moderate evidence; highest self-reported impact for episodic disability

*Source: Systematic Review of Home Modifications for Aging in Place in Older Adults (2024). PMC.*

### §11.6 Nordic Models

#### Sweden — BFS 2024:12 (Boverket)

All apartments in multi-dwelling buildings must be designed for future conversion to fully accessible without structural modification. Bathroom structural blocking: mandatory. Entry door: ≥850 mm clear. Lift: required in all buildings ≥3 storeys.

#### Norway — NS 11001-2:2018 (Standard Norge)

All new dwellings: accessible-ready specification. Multi-dwelling buildings: lift required from ground to all floors. Lever hardware, accessible electrical heights, bathroom structural support: all mandatory.

**Significance:** Both countries report significantly lower retrofit costs and higher aging-in-place rates in the decades after mandatory adaptable housing standards were adopted. The Nordic evidence provides the strongest available national-scale data for the policy value of mandatory DAR standards.

### §11.7 Case Study Evidence for DAR

For DAR provisions in practice, see the following case studies in Part 13:

- **Part 13 §13.03** (The Kelsey Civic Commons): all units built with DAR provisions; ceiling hoist blocking, bilateral grab bar blocking, and 950 mm structural door openings specified universally. Resident-reported care attendant hour reduction: 62% of respondents.
- **Part 13 §13.07** (Singapore HDB): grab bar blocking in all bathrooms; flat-to-care-home transfer rate significantly lower in UD-specified blocks vs standard blocks (longitudinal, n>5,000).
- **Part 13 §13.14** (BC Housing HAFI): programme evaluation of retrofit outcomes provides the converse evidence — average CAD 7,500 modification cost per home represents \~30--40% of what would have been required without DAR provisions.

*Next: Part 12 — The Economics of Accessible Construction* *Cross-reference: Part 1 §1.6 (DAR cost multiplier table, also in this Part §11.1); Part 12 §12.2 (macro cost ratio); Part 12 §12.4 (technique-level retrofit cost intelligence); Part 13 (case study evidence for DAR provisions in practice)*
## Part 12: The Economics of Accessible Construction

### Scope and Purpose

This part presents the economic case for building with accessibility incorporated at design stage versus achieving it through later retrofit. It is written for the design team — architects, access consultants, interior designers, project managers, and building owners — who need to think clearly about cost and value, and to articulate that thinking to clients, developers, and procurement authorities. It is not a financial modelling manual. It does not require the reader to operate cost software, apply correction factors, or perform investment appraisal. Those tools belong to the quantity surveyor and the specialist economist working alongside the design team.

This part's purpose is to equip the design professional with the right questions, the right vocabulary, and the right conceptual framework — so that economic conversations about accessibility are grounded in evidence and principle rather than assumption and habit.

The central argument is structural and asymmetric: accessibility built into a building is a specification decision, not a physical intervention. The same outcome achieved through retrofit requires physical intervention into finished structures — opening walls, cutting floors, relocating services, reinforcing structure — each multiplying cost by factors of 5 to 50 depending on the technique. The question facing every designer is therefore not whether accessibility has a cost premium, but when it is paid.

Read this part alongside Volume II (Parts 7 and 9), which describes specific design interventions. The economic principles articulated here are most clearly seen through examples drawn from the Part 7 item library; those examples are cross-referenced throughout. For DAR (Design for Adaptable Readiness) cost provisions, see Part 11.

**Key Arguments at a Glance — For Client Conversations**

- Accessibility decided at brief stage typically adds 1--4% to construction cost (or less). The same provision as a retrofit costs 10--40× more. The cheapest moment to act is now.
- Most individual accessibility provisions — loop floor plan, level entry, acoustic zoning, toilet visibility — cost nothing at schematic design because they are decisions, not materials.
- Accessible buildings are more functional: they serve more people, adapt to changing populations, and reduce future retrofit liability.
- The strongest economic argument is not financial modelling — it is the cost curve. State it clearly, cite the evidence, and the client has the information they need.
- Accessibility is not a premium. An inaccessible building is an incomplete product.

### §12.1 The Cost Myth and What the Evidence Shows

#### §12.1.1 The Assumed Burden

The dominant assumption in building procurement — across most jurisdictions and building types — is that accessibility costs money: that to make a building more accessible is to spend more. This assumption is so pervasive that it rarely surfaces as a claim to be examined. It is simply taken as given, shaping every project brief, every value-engineering session, and every conversation about what must be cut when budgets tighten. The evidence does not support this assumption, at least not in the form in which it is usually held.

#### §12.1.2 What the Evidence Actually Shows

Studies conducted across multiple languages, jurisdictions, and building types consistently reach the same finding: when accessibility is integrated from the earliest design stages, its cost in new construction is very small relative to total project value, and in many cases negligible.

| Source | Key Finding |
| --- | --- |
| TERRAGON/DStGB (Germany, 2017) | 130 of 140 DIN 18040-2 criteria = zero additional cost at design stage. Full compliance adds 0.5%--1.3% of total project cost (≈€21.50/m²). |
| Ielegems \& Vanrie (Belgium, 2024) | Universal design cost premium by building type: residential 0.94%, educational 1.67%, office 2.31%, healthcare 3.92%. Premium approaches zero after three experienced accessible-design projects. |
| Fuglerud et al. (Norway, 2015) | Strongly positive benefit-cost ratios for universal design across Norwegian building types. Core specifications add negligible cost at schematic design. |
| Rick Hansen Foundation (Canada, 2023) | Design-stage provision vs. post-occupancy retrofit: 10–40× cost differential documented across Canadian housing stock. |
| Swiss Federal Office (multiple years) | Findings consistent with German and Belgian evidence. Accessibility in new construction is a planning question, not primarily a cost question. |
*"Barrierefreiheit im Neubau ist keine Kostenfrage, sondern eine Frage | der Planung" — Bundesfachstelle Barrierefreiheit (2019). Accessibility in new construction is not a cost question; it is a planning question.*

#### §12.1.3 When Costs Appear, and Why

Cost is not intrinsic to accessibility. It is the consequence of late decision-making, inadequate brief preparation, and the separation of accessibility from mainstream design thinking. The following pattern is documented consistently across the German, Canadian, Norwegian, and French literature:

| Stage | What can be decided at zero cost | Cost of deferral |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Pre-design \& Brief | Building form, circulation strategy, programme adjacency, storey count, level-entry strategy | If not established here: redesign at each subsequent stage at multiplying cost |
| Schematic Design | Loop floor plan, corridor widths, toilet adjacency, lift shaft location, ramp geometry, structural grid | Grid changes after structural design: significant premium; lift shaft omitted: may not be correctable |
| Technical Design | Surface specifications, hearing loop routing, door hardware, grab bar blocking, HVAC acoustic treatment | Acoustic treatment to a fixed room: performance ceiling below design-stage equivalent |
| Construction | Minor surface treatments, hardware substitutions, signage | Change orders: 3–10× the equivalent brief-stage provision |
| Post-Occupancy | Assistive technology overlays, portable ramps, signage, minor hardware | Structural interventions: 10–40× design-stage equivalent (Rick Hansen Foundation 2023; KfW/Prognos) |

**The Client Conversation:** The most persuasive economic argument is not 'this investment has a positive NPV.' It is: 'If we decide this now, it costs almost nothing. If we decide it in eighteen months, it will cost many times more.' This argument requires no financial modelling --- only clarity about where in the decision timeline the client is standing.

*Part 7 cross-reference: Item A-04 (Acoustic Zoning): 'Acoustic performance begins with adjacency decisions, not materials. Once adjacencies are fixed, all subsequent acoustic interventions work under constraints and at cost.' Item A-09 (HVAC Structural Isolation): 'Correct structural isolation at construction costs approximately 3--5% of mechanical plant cost. Retrofit isolation after construction can equal or exceed the original plant cost.'*

### §12.2 The Core Ratio: New Construction vs Retrofit

#### §12.2.1 New Construction Premium

The most rigorous study available is the 2017 TERRAGON/Deutscher Städte- und Gemeindebund (DStGB) study conducted using a model five-storey residential building in Berlin with 20 dwellings and 1,500 m² of floor area.

Analysing all 140 criteria of DIN 18040-2 (the German standard for barrier-free residential buildings), the study found:

- 130 of 140 criteria require zero additional cost when incorporated at design stage — they are achieved through intelligent planning and specification alone.
- The 10 criteria that do generate cost premiums produce a total additional cost of €21.50/m² (full compliance variant) or €9.20/m² (reduced-barrier variant with ≤20mm thresholds at shower and balcony).
- For a 75 m² apartment, full barrier-free compliance adds approximately €1,600 to build cost.
- As a proportion of total project cost: 0.5%--1.3%. The Belgian study by Ielegems and Vanrie (2024), applying real building projects and combining universal design evaluation criteria with actual quantity surveyor data, found universal design premiums for new construction ranging from 0.94% to 3.92% of total construction cost depending on building type — confirming the German findings with a wider international sample.

Earlier Nordic/North American evidence is consistent: the ADA National Network (US) confirmed that accessibility features in new construction add less than 1% to project cost. The independent living advocacy literature (Ratzka, CIB W84 proceedings) cites studies from the 1970s onward reaching the same conclusion with cost premiums typically below 0.5% in planned multi-family projects.

#### §12.2.2 Retrofit Cost

The contrast with retrofit is stark. A Prognos study commissioned by KfW (Germany) found that barrier-reduction retrofits carried out under the KfW "Altersgerecht Umbauen" programme averaged €19,100 per dwelling and this figure achieved reduction of barriers, not full DIN 18040-2 compliance. Full compliance retrofit was assessed as significantly higher and often structurally impractical.

In the US context, the ADA National Network reports that retrofitting the same features added 20% to project costs.

The Rick Hansen Foundation (Canada, 2023) concluded that "even modest retrofits can cost hundreds of thousands of dollars, while proactive planning reduces those costs to pennies per square foot."

US unit-level data (Angi/HomeAdvisor, 2025--26) provides the operational picture: average basic accessibility retrofit is $4,500; comprehensive home wheelchair-accessibility renovation ranges from $18,000 to $75,000. ADA-compliant bathroom and kitchen renovations alone: $9,000--$40,000.

#### §12.2.3 The Core Ratio

| Condition | Cost Premium | Per-Dwelling Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Accessibility built in at new construction | 0.5%--1.3% of total project cost | \~€1,600 (75 m² flat, Germany) |
| Barrier reduction retrofit (KfW programme, Germany) | Avg. €19,100 per dwelling | 12× new-build provision cost |
| Full compliance retrofit (KfW programme, estimated) | Significantly above €19,100; often structurally impractical | — |
| ADA-compliant renovation (US, 2025) | $18,000–$75,000 per dwelling | Variable |

*Sources: TERRAGON/DStGB (2017); Prognos/KfW; Ielegems \& Vanrie (2024); ADA National Network; Rick Hansen Foundation (2023).*

For DAR (Design for Adaptable Readiness) construction-stage provision costs and retrofit multipliers at the individual technique level, see Part 12 §12.5 and §12.4 below

### §12.3 How to Think About Value

Capital cost is only one dimension of the economic case for accessible design, and in most circumstances it is the weakest dimension --- because capital cost is a one-time event, while the benefits of accessible design accumulate over the life of the building. Design professionals should be equipped to identify and communicate at least four dimensions of value.

#### §12.3.4 Social and Systemic Value Accessible design enables participation in employment, education, civic

life, culture, and daily commerce. Research across 40 countries (Tibble 2005; Zaidi and Burchardt 2005; Mitra and Jones 2017; Estrada-Mejia et al. 2023) estimated the extra costs imposed on households with a disabled member at approximately 5.7% of total household expenditure additional expenditures on transport, assistance, specialist equipment, and healthcare attributable to environments not designed for the full range of human bodies.

This figure has two uses in practice: as a benefit metric (accessible design that reduces the need for specialist equipment and personal assistance directly reduces these costs) and as a framing tool (inaccessible environments impose costs on disabled people; accessible design removes them). This is not primarily a financial argument. It is an ethical argument about whose needs count in the design of shared space — one codified in the CRPD, the EU Web Accessibility Directive, the Accessible Canada Act, and equivalent instruments across more than 180 countries. The economic evidence supports the ethical argument; it does not substitute for it.

*Part 7 cross-reference: Category F (Sensory Zoning — formerly Category G; see Volume 2 v8.0 resequencing note) provisions — graduated stimulation gradients, re-entry protocols, sensory rooms — are not individually expensive. Their cumulative effect is to make a building habitable for people who would otherwise be unable to remain in it. The cost of their absence is not a reduction in profit; it is an absence of access.*

#### §12.3.1 Functional Value: What the Building Can Do

An accessible building can be used by more people. This is not a peripheral benefit; it is the building's core function. A building that cannot be used by a wheelchair user, a person with low vision, a Deaf person, or a person with a cognitive disability is a building that is failing a proportion of its intended users.

The reframing matters. The question is not 'does accessibility cost too much?' It is 'what is the cost of the building's failure to function for some of its users?' An accessible building is a more complete product; the comparison is not between an accessible building and a cheaper building, but between a building that works and one that does not.

The empirical evidence for functional value is strongest in dementia design research. Post-occupancy evaluation of De Hogeweyk Dementia Village (BuroKade Architects / Vivium Care Group, Dutch-language POE reports, 2012--2019) found that 94% of residents with moderate dementia could independently navigate to the village square and back within six months of admission, compared with 34% in conventional dementia care units. De Hogeweyk also operated at a lower per-resident staffing ratio than conventional Dutch facilities despite higher measured quality-of-life scores — providing economic evidence that environmental quality reduces care dependency.

#### §12.3.2 Lifecycle Value: What the Building Costs to Own and Operate

Buildings are occupied, maintained, adapted, and replaced over periods of twenty to a hundred years. Accessible design contributes to lifecycle value through three mechanisms:

**Adaptability:** Buildings designed to accommodate the full range of human bodies do not need substantial modification when the user population changes — when a workforce ages, when a tenant with a disability joins, when a building changes use. This reduces lifecycle adaptation cost.

**Maintenance durability:** Many accessible design elements — wider corridors, robust surfaces, level transitions, simple wayfinding — are less demanding to maintain, because they reduce the wear patterns that arise when people work around inaccessible features.

**Regulatory durability:** Accessibility standards are generally moving towards greater coverage and higher performance requirements. Buildings designed to best practice today will require less adaptation to meet tomorrow's standards. The German KfW/Prognos evaluation of the Altersgerecht Umbauen programme — which subsidised over 700,000 dwelling retrofits between 2009 and 2022 — found an average retrofit cost of €19,100 per dwelling for barrier-reduction measures that did not achieve full DIN 18040-2 compliance. This is the cost of the regulatory-durability gap.

#### §12.3.3 Market and Institutional Value

In commercial and institutional markets, accessible design increasingly functions as a competitive differentiator:

- Elevator retrofitting has been found to produce a measured housing price increase. Research on Hangzhou residential compounds (Buildings, 2024) found a 5.53% mean price increase overall. Single-city study; treat as jurisdiction-specific, not generalisable without corroborating evidence.
- Buildings with strong ESG performance credentials have in some markets demonstrated premium performance. The size and generalisability of an accessibility-specific rent premium is not established in the current literature and should not be stated without a verified source.
- The population of disabled people and older adults who benefit from accessible design constitutes 15--20% of the population in developed economies — a substantial and historically underserved consumer segment with material spending power.
- Non-compliance with accessibility legislation creates financial exposures: ADA Title III litigation costs, UK Equality Act Section 20 remediation orders, and CRPD complaint mechanisms are all real financial risks that design-stage accessible provision avoids.

### §12.4 Construction Cost Intelligence Tables

The following tables provide design-stage cost intelligence for accessible design techniques, expressed as percentage of project cost (new construction premium) and retrofit multiplier (× new construction cost). This format is used in place of absolute unit costs, which vary by a factor of 4× or more across target jurisdictions and provide false precision without local calibration.

**How to read these tables:**

| Column | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| New Construction Premium | Additional cost vs. non-accessible equivalent when specified at brief/schematic stage. Zero = no extra cost at design stage. |
| Retrofit Multiplier (×NC) | How many times more expensive when retrofitted post-occupancy. Multiply your NC cost by this figure. |
| Decision Stage | Latest stage at which NC cost still applies. After this, retrofit pricing begins. |
*Calibration sources: TERRAGON/DStGB (2017); Ielegems \& Vanrie (2024); |Rick Hansen Foundation (2023); KfW/Prognos (2022); CEREMA (2023); Fuglerud et al. (2015); Part I §1.6 DAR cost data (CAN/ASC 2.8:2025 (current as of January 2026); Habinteg 2010).*

#### §12.4.1 Circulation \& Entry

| Technique | Part 7 Ref | NC Premium | Retrofit ×NC | Decision Stage |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
**| Level entry / no-step threshold | E-06 | \*\*0%\*\* | 8–15× | Brief |** 
**| Corridor width ≥1200mm | E-08 | \*\*0–0.3%\*\* | 4–10× | Schematic | | Corridor width ≥1500mm (passing space) | E-08 | \*\*0.1–0.5%\*\* | 5–12× | Schematic |** 
**| Door clear opening ≥850mm | E-03 | \*\*0%\*\* | 3–8× | Technical design |** 
**| Automatic door operator | E-04/E-11 | \*\*0.1–0.3% per door\*\* | 1.5–2.5× | Technical design |** 
**| Lift/elevator shaft provision (structure only) | E-01/E-02 | \*\*0.5–1.5%\*\* | \*\*20–40×\*\* | \*\*Brief\*\* |** 
**| Lift/elevator installation (fit-out) | E-01/E-02 | \*\*2–5% (residential)\*\* | 3–6× | Technical design | | Platform lift (≤3m rise) | E-02 | \*\*0.3–1%\*\* | 4–8× | Technical design |** 
**| Accessible parking bay (1:25 ratio) | E-04 | \*\*0%\*\* | 2–4× | Schematic |** 
**| Tactile ground surface indicators | E-09 | \*\*0–0.1%\*\* | 2–3× | Construction |** 
**| Accessible signage / wayfinding system | D-06/D-08 | \*\*0–0.1%\*\* | 1.5–2× | Construction | | Rest seating at ≤30m intervals | E-10 | \*\*0–0.1%\*\* | 2–4× | Schematic |** 
| Covered entrance canopy ≥3000×2000mm | E-05 | **0.1–0.3%** | 3–6× | Schematic |
**Critical note — lift shaft:** Omission of lift shaft from the | structural design is the single highest-consequence accessibility deferral documented in the evidence base. Specify structural provision at brief stage regardless of immediate fit-out budget.

#### §12.4.2 Toilet and Bathroom Facilities

| Technique | Part 7 Ref | NC Premium | Retrofit ×NC | Decision Stage |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
**| Ambulant accessible WC (enlarged cubicle) | G-01 | \*\*0.1–0.3%\*\* | 3–6× | Schematic |** 
**| Wheelchair accessible unisex WC | G-02 | \*\*0.3–0.8%\*\* | 6–15× | Schematic | | Grab bar blocking in walls (residential) | G-03 | \*\*0%\*\* | 4–8× | Construction |** 
**| Roll-in / level access / zero-threshold shower | G-04 | \*\*0.2–0.5%\*\* | 5–12× | Technical design |** 
**| Height-adjustable fixtures | G-05 | \*\*0.3–0.8%\*\* | 2–4× | Technical design |** 
**| Type B (visitability) bathroom — residential | G-04/G-03 | \*\*0.5–1.0%\*\* | 8–20× | Schematic |** 
**| Changing Places facility | G-07 | \*\*0.8–1.5%\*\* | 15–30× | Brief |** 
| Heated bathroom floor (fall/heat shock prevention) | TC-05 | **0.2–0.4%** | 3–5× | Technical design |

**Evidence note:** The bathroom is the highest-risk location in domestic environments for both falls and cardiovascular events. Japanese epidemiological data documents ≈17,000 cardiovascular deaths per year attributable to bathroom temperature differential >10°C. Threshold height ≥15mm is documented as statistically significant fall risk increase (AIJ). Both provisions are zero-additional-cost at design stage.

#### §12.4.3 Acoustic Design

| Technique | Part 7 Ref | NC Premium | Retrofit ×NC | Decision Stage |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
**| Acoustic zoning (plan layout) | A-04 | \*\*0%\*\* | 3–8× | Schematic | | Airborne sound insulation (Rw ≥50dB party walls) | A-14 | \*\*0.3–0.8%\*\* | 4–10× | Technical design |** 
**| Impact sound insulation (floating floor) | A-03 | \*\*0.2–0.6%\*\* | 5–12× | Construction |** 
**| Room reverberation control (RT ≤0.4s) | A-02/A-05 | \*\*0.3–0.7%\*\* | 3–6× | Technical design |** 
**| Hearing loop — perimeter (room) | A-11 | \*\*0.1–0.3%\*\* | 2–4× | Technical design |** 
**| Hearing loop — counter | A-10 | \*\*0–0.1%\*\* | 1.5–2.5× | Construction | | Background noise ≤35dB(A) (HVAC spec) | A-08/A-09 | \*\*0.2–0.5%\*\* | 2–4× | Technical design |** 
| Sensory room (RT ≤0.3s; individual control) | A-16 | **0.5–1.2%** | 3–5× | Schematic |

**System integrity note:** Acoustic accessibility provisions function as systems. Removing a single item from an integrated acoustic system (e.g. removing ceiling treatment while retaining hearing loop) often destroys more value than the cost saved. Brief acoustic accessibility as an outcome system, not as independent line items. See §12.5.2.

#### §12.4.4 Visual Environment \& Lighting

| Technique | Part 7 Ref | NC Premium | Retrofit ×NC | Decision Stage |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
**| Tonal contrast at circulation edges (LRV ≥30 diff.) | C-04 | \*\*0%\*\* | 0.5–1× | Construction |** 
**| High-CRI lighting (CRI ≥90) | B-02 | \*\*0.1–0.3%\*\* | 1–2× | Technical design |** 
**| Glare-controlled luminaires | B-03/B-04 | \*\*0.1–0.2%\*\* | 1–2× | Technical design |** 
**| Illuminance ≥300 lux (task areas) | B-04 | \*\*0–0.2%\*\* | 1–1.5× | Technical design |** 
**| Daylight — window head height ≥2100mm | B-09 | \*\*0%\*\* | N/A | Schematic |** 
**| Circadian lighting (variable CCT) | B-01/B-11 | \*\*0.3–0.8%\*\* | 1.5–2.5× | Technical design |** 
**| Visual fire alarm throughout | B-10 | \*\*0.1–0.2%\*\* | 1.5–3× | Technical design |** 
| Colour-coded zones | C-02 | **0%** | 0.5–1× | Construction |  |

#### §12.4.5 Cognitive Accessibility \& Wayfinding

| Technique | Part 7 Ref | NC Premium | Retrofit ×NC | Decision Stage |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Loop floor plan (dementia / orientation) | D-01 | **0%** | 8–20× | **Brief** |
| Toilet visible from primary living space | D-03 | **0%** | Not retrofittable | **Schematic** |
| Visual access to destination from entry | D-02/D-10 | **0%** | 5–15× | Schematic |
| Landmark objects at decision points | D-04 | **0–0.1%** | 1–2× | Construction |
**| Pictogram + word signage at junctions | D-06/D-08 | \*\*0–0.1%\*\* | 1–1.5× | Construction |** 
**| Quiet / low-stimulus retreat space | D-05/A-16 | \*\*0.1–0.3%\*\* | 3–6× | Schematic |**

| Secured outdoor loop path (dementia) | D-11 | **0.1–0.3%** | 5–10× | Schematic |

**Critical note — toilet visibility:** Toilet visibility from the primary living space in a dementia care unit is a schematic design decision — it determines the relationship between room positions. It cannot be retrofitted. Marquardt (2011, 32-facility study): 47% reduction in incontinence events. This is among the strongest single empirical findings for any spatial provision in this Guidebook.

**| Thermal mass | TC-03 | \*\*0%\*\* | N/A | Brief |** 
| Air quality monitoring and ventilation | A-08/TC-04 | **0.2–0.5%** | 2–3× | Technical design |  |
